need to save a lot of space and power, they're not a bargain.

From iconoklastic at yahoo.com  Thu Jan  3 00:26:36 2008
From: iconoklastic at yahoo.com (Robert Kopp)
Date: Thu, 3 Jan 2008 00:26:36 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] NFS-DHCP?
Message-ID: <476138.59057.qm@web50611.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

I know next to nothing about what I am attempting to do, so I thought I'd ask for advice. I want to use a Linux computer (SuSE 10.3) as an NFS server, and it normally receives an IP address from a 2700HG-D "Internet gateway" by DHCP. When I assign a static IP to the (sole) network card on the server, it seems to have problems.

At the same time, a NFS client should have a line something like this in fstab:

<server>:/home  /mnt/home  nfs  rw  0  0

which, by its very nature, entails that <server> does not change. Anyhow, it occurred to me that if I assigned an alias to the server's network card, that address would not change between boots and could be used for this purpose (at least, on a network with no more than a few computers). It this a tenable approach?


Robert "Tim" Kopp
http://analytic.tripod.com/

From russell at personaltelco.net  Thu Jan  3 01:14:57 2008
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 03 Jan 2008 01:14:57 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] NFS-DHCP?
In-Reply-To: <476138.59057.qm@web50611.mail.re2.yahoo.com>
References: <476138.59057.qm@web50611.mail.re2.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <86myrn8e3y.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Robert" == Robert Kopp <iconoklastic at yahoo.com> writes:

Robert> I know next to nothing about what I am attempting to do, so I
Robert> thought I'd ask for advice. I want to use a Linux computer
Robert> (SuSE 10.3) as an NFS server, and it normally receives an IP
Robert> address from a 2700HG-D "Internet gateway" by DHCP. When I
Robert> assign a static IP to the (sole) network card on the server,
Robert> it seems to have problems.  At the same time, a NFS client
Robert> should have a line something like this in fstab:

Robert> <server>:/home /mnt/home nfs rw 0 0

Robert> which, by its very nature, entails that <server> does not
Robert> change. Anyhow, it occurred to me that if I assigned an alias
Robert> to the server's network card, that address would not change
Robert> between boots and could be used for this purpose (at least, on
Robert> a network with no more than a few computers). It this a
Robert> tenable approach?

That might work.  But better would be to just fix the network
configuration problem.  I don't know what it means "seems to have
problems".  I'm guessing that DNS and/or default gateway or something
isn't getting set up when you configure statically.  The other option
would be to tell the DHCP server to always give out the same address
to your server.  I have no idea wthat a 2700HG-D is though, so I can't
give step-by-step instructions.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary

Richard Lidzbarski wrote:
> Here are some excerpts from the BBC:
> 
> "The chip maker has been promoting its own cheap laptop, the Classmate, in 
> many of the same places as the OLPC."
> 
> "He added that the use of AMD chips in the first XO laptops had not influenced 
> its decision."
> 
> "Prior to Intel's involvement, OLPC founder Nicholas Negroponte criticised the

> 
> 
> I say good riddance to Intel...OLPC will be better off without them...or

Well ... OLPC can't compete with Intel and Microsoft. As you may know, I
have one of the OLPC laptops as part of the Give One Get One program
that just ended. So I'm on most of the OLPC mailing lists.

I really feel sorry for the OLPC people. First, Intel developed and
*aggressively* marketed -- *really* aggressively -- a competing product.
Then OLPC negotiated some kind of deal with them, and Intel joined in
the project. There was some engineering -- how much, I don't know -- on
an OLPC laptop with Intel chips. And now Intel pulled out of the deal.
That sucks, but then again, that's what near-monopolies do.

1st, You had troubles the last time you released the iso to just a 
couple people on the list, and now you post it to digg?

2nd, the digg link takes me to an empty sf page. Is this intentional? 
Why the detour?

3rd, the download link on your site takes me to an Adobe Flash EULA?

   http://www.mindblowingidea.com/AdobeFlashEULA.html

4th, your "2nd mirror" link says it "requires" FF, and won't work in IE. 
  Well, it works in IE. Yes I'm one of those tell me what I can't do and 
I will try to do it types.

5th, the 0.1.1.1 torrent link ref's a local drive path instead of an 
actual server address people can get to, and your "FTP Download" link is 
broke, and it actually points to an http link anyway. </run-on>

5th, your video is depressing. Are you playing air-keyboard at the 
beginning? You probably shouldn't promote /your/ product by talking 
about what you were unable get other operating systems you've used to 
do. Rather, tell people what yours "can" do. I hate negative ads. And 
don't say stuff like "all it is, is PC Linux w/ a couple changes". It's 
almost as if you're talking me out of downloading it. I stopped watching 
there.

This comes across as harsh, I know. This is just what I saw getting to 
the download link though, and I could go on. You don't need some 
grandiose site with bling and all that shiz. But what you do have 
doesn't make it look like you take this seriously in the least, or that 
you are competent enough to put something complicated like an OS 
together when your web pages and links are in the shape their in. Maybe 
the OS shows a different story and your skills will blow people away. 
But no one is going to get there. Take this as constructive criticism. 
I don't know you, it's nothing personal.

-- 
- m0gely

Yes your Linux Firefox bookmarks should be in your home dir.

But there is an easier way to do this, once which I have been doing since
running 64bit Linux (Fedora), is to use the 32bit Firefox to be able to use
all the plugins (Java, Flash, etc) that are not 64bit.  This way you don't
have to mess with wine.  I am not positive but you should be able to just
download the 32bit Firefox RPM and install it, depending on if you have the
32bit compatibility libraries installed.  Just be careful when you do YAST
updates that it updates the 32bit one and not overwriting with the 64bit one
again, you may have to do that manually.





-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight


From johnxj at comcast.net  Tue Jan  8 09:40:42 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Tue, 8 Jan 2008 09:40:42 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Where are my bookmarks?
In-Reply-To: <396511.6056.qm@web50602.mail.re2.yahoo.com>
References: <396511.6056.qm@web50602.mail.re2.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20080108094042.f8697f99.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Tue, 8 Jan 2008 00:37:52 -0800 (PST)
Robert Kopp <iconoklastic at yahoo.com> dijo:

> I am running a 64-bit version of Linux, specifically SuSE 10.3. As expected, Java did not appear in Firefox, and I resolved to do something about it. The easiest way to solve it is to install the Windows version of Firefox using WINE.
> 
> This worked so well that it created another problem: now I can't seem to get back into the Linux version of Firefox, where my bookmarks are. Can anyone give me advice on this?

If you want to use Firefox/Linux and have the same bookmarks, you can
export the bookmarks from the Windows version (which it creates as
an .html file), then import them into Firefox/Linux. You can also do
this with Opera, and perhaps other browsers as well.

FWIW I use 64-bit Firefox on Ubuntu Gutsy x86_64 and I have all the
plugins working just fine (Flash, Java, Acroread, etc.). It may be
easier to use 32-bit Firefox to get the plugins, but it is no longer
the only way.

That's one reason i use Adobe's product and 32-bit Firefox - the 
acroread plugin displays pdfs in the browser, which i find quite 
convenient. It's also impressively identical across platforms, thanks (i 
believe) to the fact that it's developed with Qt. Also,  in my 
experience Acroread does a cleaner job of printing than evince - at 
least acroread v.8 does, the earlier versions for Linux weren't so hot.

i'm admittedly not an open source purist - even though i've switched to 
Ubutntu for almost all my personal & development work, i use an iPod and 
thus have mp3 codecs loaded so i can use my existing music library 
rhythmbox, i dual-boot to Vista to run Quicken & TurboTax and to stay 
current with the state of pain that is Windows, etc.

If i could only get suspend & hibernate working...

Joe

You should be able to run 32 bit applications on 64 bit systems by 
loading the compat libraries:

compat-libgcc-296-2.96-138.x86_64.rpm
or
compat-libgcc-34-3.4.6-4.x86_64.rpm

Fedora
yum install compat-libgcc
should get you the correct version for your installation.

For SuSE
apt-get install compat-2004 compat-sles8


Ken Stephens
CAD 2 CAM


From nickaubert at gmail.com  Wed Jan  9 10:26:49 2008
From: nickaubert at gmail.com (Nick Aubert)
Date: Wed, 9 Jan 2008 10:26:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] 64-bit Firefox foibles
In-Reply-To: <4784286C.6070002@easystreet.net>
References: <mailman.5.1199822404.24108.plug@lists.pdxlinux.org>
	<courier.4783E697.00002F0B@softhome.net>
	<47841E29.1080706@schluting.com> <4784286C.6070002@easystreet.net>
Message-ID: <55ef240d0801091026r4dea5e68n8fbe7ec820714bad@mail.gmail.com>

The PDF Download plugin for Firefox (http://www.pdfdownload.org) will
automatically load the document in the PDF viewer of your choice.
That's what I use since PDFs break the flow of the web anyway.
There's an option to have the plugin convert the PDF to HTML, and then
you can have the document in a browser tab.  Unfortunately it has to
send the PDF to the developers' server for conversion, so you view it
as a page from their site.  If this doesn't bother you, that's one way
to do it.

-Nick

We had the same situation last summer on one of our trips to British
Columbia looking for work.  On this particular trip, our motel of
choice in Surrey, BC had wifi, but we couldn't get an IP.  So we
talked to the front counter, they cut us a big discount for the night
and got a technician out to figure out what was wrong, then went to go
get some dinner, local transit information and get properly settled
for the week.  The next morning, I woke up, was able to connect and
get some emails out to let potential employers know I was in town and
available for a few days.  Sometimes it pays to let 'em know about the
problem, too...

References: <47854048.80100@seitzassoc.com>	<15469.198.182.194.170.1199916407.squirrel@clueserver.org>	<d0ad9b7e0801091502g4d53ba30n5a9a0c0a89faca49@mail.gmail.com>	<7.0.1.0.0.20080109151955.05655d50@ipns.com>

Paul Johnson wrote:

> Seems like a better answer would be to get a higher gain antenna...

That increases their transmit power and your machine's ability to see 
the AP better. It doesn't help communication strength going *too* the 
router though.  High gain antenna's don't help much unless they are on 
both ends.

- m0gely


From russell at personaltelco.net  Wed Jan  9 22:28:35 2008
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 09 Jan 2008 22:28:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Metrofi wins Rogue of the Week
In-Reply-To: <4785AD88.3010904@telestream.com>
References: <47854048.80100@seitzassoc.com>
	<15469.198.182.194.170.1199916407.squirrel@clueserver.org>
	<d0ad9b7e0801091502g4d53ba30n5a9a0c0a89faca49@mail.gmail.com>
	<7.0.1.0.0.20080109151955.05655d50@ipns.com>
	<143e384f0801091713l7838b501u16a0c5e8f037c4e@mail.gmail.com>
	<4785AD88.3010904@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <86r6gqkxd8.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "m0gely" == m0gely  <m0gely at telestream.com> writes:

m0gely> Paul Johnson wrote:
>> Seems like a better answer would be to get a higher gain antenna...

m0gely> That increases their transmit power and your machine's ability
m0gely> to see the AP better. It doesn't help communication strength
m0gely> going *too* the router though.  High gain antenna's don't help
m0gely> much unless they are on both ends.

That's wrong, actually.  Antenna gain in bi-directional, it focuses
incoming and outgoing signal.  Transmit power is not bi-directional.
MetroFi's loud radios don't do a damn thing to make client radios
easier to hear (rather the reverse, by flooding the city with radio
noise).

So, if you are in a fixed-wireless (where neither end is moving)
situation, a dB of antenna gain is better than a dB of transmit power.
Amongst other things, you are generating less noise for everyone else.
If you are moving, though, too much antenna gain ends up being too
much of a pain to aim.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

how he was sending messages across bodies of water in late 1800's, so it's a

is out to make a buck selling little piece of shit routers with measly
radii. hahahaha.

not very educated on wireless but isn't the restricted radius of an AP have
to do with the higher band frequencies? if so, why not just use a lower
band? maybe this isn't the place for this discussion, but i figure it's
worth a try.

>>>>> "kurt" == kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> writes:

kurt> i was going to order a comcast subscription to my house, and i'd
kurt> like to share it with all my neighbors if possible. so maybe
kurt> that would be a good start for me and personaltelco. as long as
kurt> i can allocate enough bandwidth for each user (that's not
kurt> ssssssllllllllloooooooowwwwwww, i don't see any problem doing
kurt> that. is Comcast evil? who isn't? do i have a choice?

Comcast is evil in the sense that they have terms of service that say
you should not share your connection.  There are several DSL ISPs that
are explicitly sharing friendly, e.g.:

  Stephouse Networks
  Easystreet
  Speakeasy
  Integra (if you have a business line)

There may be others (if so, please ping me privately, and I'll start
plugging you too).

I am sure that some Comcast subscribers do share their connection, but
there is always the risk that if you do that Comcast will come to you
and request that you stop sending them $56/month.  We recommend that
you send your money to bandwidth providers that are friendly and
far-sighted from the get-go.

From russell at personaltelco.net  Thu Jan 10 02:00:12 2008
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 10 Jan 2008 02:00:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Metrofi wins Rogue of the Week
In-Reply-To: <422ec1510801100002r3e548f45yd86acadfe47e40f7@mail.gmail.com>
References: <47854048.80100@seitzassoc.com>
	<422ec1510801091650o245b4dc5sa38818b66ee18af5@mail.gmail.com>
	<668dd2c20801091654i27fb5b7cp8889c2d9f34446b9@mail.gmail.com>
	<867iiio4f7.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<422ec1510801100002r3e548f45yd86acadfe47e40f7@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <86abneknkj.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "kurt" == kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> writes:

kurt> not very educated on wireless but isn't the restricted radius of
kurt> an AP have to do with the higher band frequencies? if so, why
kurt> not just use a lower band? maybe this isn't the place for this
kurt> discussion, but i figure it's worth a try.

The "radius" is a function, usually, of stuff in the way.  People have
done 2.4GHz links without amplifiers of a hundred miles or more (using
very high-gain antennas).  However, throw a few trees or stucco or
metal siding or this or that or the other thing in the way, and your
mileage may vary considerably.  Theoretically, you can overcome some
of that attenuation by transmitting at a higher power, but the FCC has
limits on the power you are allowed to transmit in the "unlicensed"
bands, so that doesn't get you very far.

You are correct that lower frequency radio waves penetrate various
materials better.  Some people still use 900 MHz for data
communications, however there are some trade-offs: a) it has less data
carrying capacity; and b) nobody's laptop comes with a 900 MHz radio
built into it.

If you are interested in wireless networks, there is a nice free book
that can give you a lot of background:

  <http://wndw.net/>



-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Jan 10 10:17:40 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 10 Jan 2008 10:17:40 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Metrofi does not connect (was Rogue of the week)
In-Reply-To: <15469.198.182.194.170.1199916407.squirrel@clueserver.org>
References: <47854048.80100@seitzassoc.com>
	<15469.198.182.194.170.1199916407.squirrel@clueserver.org>
Message-ID: <20080110181740.GA2209@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, Jan 09, 2008 at 02:06:47PM -0800, Alan wrote:
> > Chief rabble-rouser Russell puts Metrofi in the spotlight.
> >    http://wweek.com/editorial/3409/10212/
> 
> My laptop can see a MetroFi node from my desk at work.  I have never been
> able to get an IP address from it or connect in any usable way.
> 
> Amazingly bogus crap.

What MetroFi promises to do - blanket a city, outdoors and by implication
indoors, with 802.11B wireless access - is probably technically
impossible.  You would probably need as many access points as there
are light bulbs in the city now, for similar reasons. 

As far as "you can see them, but you can't connect", though, the
reasons are similar to the light bulb analogy.  You can see a street
light from a long way away, but it is a lot less likely that someone
at the street light can see *you* from a long way away.  Each Metrofi
radio is greater than 100mW (200mW? 500mW?) and only transmits into a
flattened cone of space, while typical built-in Wifi is a meager 5mW
and spews energy in all directions.  So your laptop may be effectively
invisible to any access point at an appreciable distance (say, farther
away than 10 feet, through walls, trees, or above or below the cone).
They deliver more radio power to you than you do to them.

You may have more luck with a PCMCIA wireless card (some PRISM2 cards
are 200mW), if you can find one with a proper chipset and Linux drivers. 
Or maybe not, no cards are as powerful and directed as the Metrofi
antennas.  Adding a large dish antenna to the card and pointing it at
the Metrofi antenna might be enough.  That may violate some FCC rules.

Connection is not nearly as much a problem with a typical PTP node (the 
access points are indoors in specific places, mostly, in the same room
as you are), or with a Meraki mesh (zillions of cheap access points, if
one can't see you, others probably can).  But those are limited area
coverage, hundreds of square feet rather than hundreds of square miles.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"

>>>>> "Paul" == Paul Johnson <baloo at ursine.ca> writes:

Russell> As I mentioned before, 900MHz is also unlicensed.  You can
Russell> buy miniPCI cards for 900MHz pretty easily and run 802.11
Russell> over them (I've never personally done it, but we were
Russell> considering it once).  I seem to recall that madwifi
Russell> supported them.

Here is an example:

  http://www.wlanparts.com/c=f1tqBujctvTSmD8tHw46BZiwf/product/XR9/Ubiquiti_XR9_XtremeRange9_900MHz_MiniPCI_Radio.html

Paul> That's pretty interesting...also could add a layer of obscurity
Paul> if you're trying to keep your wifi network away from people
Paul> stumbling for wifi.

As someone who stumbles for wifi (ie "maps wifi networks"), I can give
a crap about anyone's individual network.  If anyone wants free
bandwidth, they only have to stumble about 50 feet before they find
it.  There are open access points virtually *everywhere*.  If you
(speaking generally) think someone is going to drive across town
because you have an open access point then you are ill-informed.
There is no need.  It is everywhere, in a city like Portland at insane
densities.  If you drive a few miles in Portland you are going to hear
thousands and thousands of networks.  I have been stumbling
intermittently for almost 2 years, mostly right here in Portland.  I
have heard as of today over 117,000 BSSID's (think wifi macaddrs).  I
estimate that at least 100 thousand of those were from here in the
Portland area.

I stumble for two reasons: a) audit networks (find specific networks
like MetroFi or Personal Telco); or b) get a sense of network density.
Well, okay, c) some of the network names show some human creativity
that are good for a chortle.  And d) if you do it from a bike it is
good exercise.

Now, if someone has a specific vendetta against you, or if you do
silly things like share windows files on your internal network that is
bridged with your wireless, then you might compensate for that
silliness by "locking down" your wireless, but I agree with Bruce
Schneier: closing your wireless network isn't polite.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

From mconnors1 at gmail.com  Thu Jan 10 13:58:53 2008
From: mconnors1 at gmail.com (Mike Connors)
Date: Thu, 10 Jan 2008 13:58:53 -0800

References: <e522eca06b18.47858213@pcc.edu>	<422ec1510801092336q7ab52d0k80459b5202a05476@mail.gmail.com>	<ebdfd14b0801100755t68ee8bgf8edafcccf505549@mail.gmail.com>
	<55ef240d0801101153l63eeeb7cv8663e3768d9aee5@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4786951D.4020800@gmail.com>

There's not a 10 limit person limit. But, if you choose the bookshelf 
option for $220/yr, you only get 10 "slots" or credits for books. Most 
books only use 1 slot, but some do use more. So with that option, if you 
had more than 10 people everyone might not get equal access to the books 
they want. Because when you put a book on the shelf it takes up a 
slot(s) for a default amount of time. I think it's 30 days or so.

If you go with the unlimited library option for $439/yr then the more 
people the cheaper it is and then everyone potentially gets equal 
access. I say this because I'm not entirely sure if you can have 20 
people logged in at the same time on the same account. It wouldn't make 
good business sense for O'Reilly to allow people to do this.

I used the 10 slot bookshelf option with a total of 7 people and I 
personally never ran into any access issues.

From creswick at gmail.com  Thu Jan 10 14:14:36 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Thu, 10 Jan 2008 14:14:36 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Top-Posting (was: Re:  Metrofi wins Rogue of the Week)
Message-ID: <c834ec80801101414v6f0301cdi19345243aee1f08a@mail.gmail.com>

On Jan 10, 2008 1:58 PM, kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> wrote:
> On Jan 10, 2008 7:53 AM, Paul Johnson <baloo at ursine.ca> wrote:
> >Please don't top-post...
>
> if i knew what a top-post was, and if i knew why it was bad, i wouldn't do
> it. sorry to ruffle your feathers.

Top-posting is the practice of replying to an email without quoting
the original for context.  This message, and your message quoted above
are formatted "properly".  You are able to see the progression of the
conversation as you read the email from the top-to-bottom.

You generally have to scan the entire email to find the relevant
portions when some one top-posts.  It's a bit like playing Jeopardy:

A: Because it makes conversations hard to follow!
Q: Why shouldn't you top post?

There are handful of other reasons -- it's just polite to make thinks
easier for everyone else, for example, but I think the disjointed
conversation flow that results from top-posting is the biggest issue.

--Rogan

"Paul Johnson" <baloo at ursine.ca> dijo:

> But which has a good Mbps/$ ratio?  If you're getting fewer Mbps than
> Comcast for the same or more money than Comcast, they suck
> automatically.

You know, I've been reading this thread for a while now. I have Comcast
at home for basic TV and internet, but I don't know what speed I'm
actually getting. If I'm downloading a torrent then Ktorrent tells me
or, if I'm using Firefox, the download manager pops up and tells me.
And in those cases I rarely see anything over 1000, although I have
seen as high as 2300 for brief periods. I have no idea what speeds I'm
getting on stuff like loading web pages, downloading e-mail, playing
streaming radio, watching youtube, etc.

It's kind of difficult to decide I should drop Comcast in favor of
something else when I don't even know what I am currently getting. Is
there a tool I can use that will monitor my internet connection, log
the speeds over a period of time, and give me a summary like "average,"
"high" "low," and so on?

I imagine it will be a good long while before *any* email client will
know where to put your cursor when you start a reply ;)  Given the
choice between starting at the top, bottom, or some random location in
the middle, I'll choose one of the first two any time.  (I actually
have a slight preference for starting at the top, since it makes it
easier to scroll down to the place I actually want to quote.)

Hm... I wonder if a paradigm where you format the reply while reading
the email (similar to word's track-changes -- so you can see what
you've added / deleted) and then choose the recipients, would work for
people.   As long as a pristine version of the original email is kept,
and accessible, I don't see any reason to keep *any* email immutable.

--Rogan

On Thu, 10 Jan 2008, Rogan Creswick wrote:

> I imagine it will be a good long while before *any* email client will
> know where to put your cursor when you start a reply ;)

   They all put the cursor at the upper left corner. The issue is that too
many folks just start typing there and don't make the effort to trim cruft,
isolate portions of the original message to which they want to respond, then
type their response beneath that quoted text.

   It's like html-formatted mail: when all your friends and colleages top
post and send html mail, why bother being different? There are no tests and
licenses required before driving on the information highway.

> "Paul Johnson" <baloo at ursine.ca> dijo:
> It's kind of difficult to decide I should drop Comcast in favor of
> something else when I don't even know what I am currently getting. Is
> there a tool I can use that will monitor my internet connection, log

My ISP hosts a few test files for downloading from their servers as a 
handy real-world test. Online test tools encounter the confounding 
factor of internet traffic to the tool's host, but you can average 
results taken at different times. Take a look here:

http://www.dslreports.com/speedtest

On Thu, 10 Jan 2008 18:07:09 -0800
merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz) dijo:

> I *regularly* get 20 megabit down, 1.5 megabit up, on my residential comcast
> link, out here in Aloha-land.  It's a bit slower during prime time, but not
> much.

Using speedtest.net and the flash tests from dslreports.com/speedtest,
recommended by Joe and Mark respectively, it seems the fastest I am
getting is around 23,500 Kb/s down and about 2000 Kb/s up. In the real
world, however, I never see anything like that speed for downloads -
generally the best I ever see is about a tenth of that, and even that
is very unusual. I assume the bottleneck is the server I am downloading
from. And if that is the bottleneck, then getting a faster ISP would be
pointless. The only reason for switching from Comcast is because they
are nasty and dishonest. That may be reason enough, but I have to weigh
the cost, which would be substantial.

I did check out DSL about a month ago (called Spiretech, who I used for
dialup years ago) and was informed that my address qualified for the
highest speed possible (although I do not recall what it was). However,
I would have to buy telephone service (at least the basic without
voice), plus pay an ISP, and then I would still need Comcast for
television, unless I invest in Direct TV or something else. I recall
that when I did the math it would end up costing me about $80 a month,
compared to the $55 I now pay Comcast, plus having to buy a new modem
and add a phone line inside the house. That's quite a lot just to be
able to tell Comcast to stick it.

If my reasoning is faulty, I'm sure someone will correct me. :)

with an explaination that they can't charge me for services not
rendered.  Odds are Qwest won't get off their fat asses tomorrow to
fix it, either.  Their loss.  My Verizon experience is similar,
average wait for repair when I had them was 165 days.

On Jan 10, 2008 5:08 PM, Mike Connors <mconnors1 at gmail.com> wrote:
> Actually, Thunderbird puts the cursor below the original email text.
> Also, being a n00b to email lists, I wasn't aware of proper posting
> etiquette.

It's not exactly limited to just mailing lists.  The expectation
extends to Usenet and private email as well.  You should not expect
your audience to have necessarily received your previous message, or
may be looking back at it in an archive.

> And personally, I prefer the newest posts at the top so I
> don't have to scroll down past all of the posts I've already read.

If you have to scroll to get to new material, you're including too
much text or not preserving context.  Break it up or remove
unnecessary portions before sending.

On Jan 10, 2008 7:28 PM, Mike Connors <mconnors1 at gmail.com> wrote:

> In the age of co-ops, maybe it's time for the first co-op ISP right here
> in Portland. I know there's a community colocation project in San
> Francisco. http://www.sfccp.net/
> Then we can tell them all to go stick it! Whos's with me?

Uhh...

The second co-op ISP was right here in Portland, Rain Net.  Around
1985 IIRC.  The first was "The Little Garden" in Palo Alto a few
months earlier.

We brought the first "high speed" internet into the Portland area,
through a T1 line from Mae West into Randy Bush's house, then divided
it down from there with modems.  Split it out further by various other
sites;  I fed from machines at Alan Batie's house (later to become
Aracnet).  Other secondary Rain Net sites became Pacifier, and Teleport
(bought by Earthlink).   At one point, Portland was the best connected
city on the planet (we had a few hundred active machines).  The bay
area had more, but spread among a lot larger population.  History
repeated itself with the Personal Telco Project;  Portland led the
world again ( What's next? ).

When Rain Net became too much for Randy to handle, it went
professional.  My friend Robert Chew managed it for few years, as
it grew to dozens of employees.  Then Rain Net got bought and bought
again.  I think they are currently part of Nippon Telephone, and only
handle business customers (I had a class C network, 256 routable IP
addresses, before the suits kicked me out).
 
Providers like Comcast and Qwest and Verizon are quite recent.

Most of the business ended up with corporate providers because running
an ISP is a lot of work, and the economies of scale are massive.  My
sister and her ex-husband were running LavaNet in Honolulu, and the
strain destroyed their marriage.  

History repeats itself.  Read the history of Theodore Vail and the
Bell Telephone network.  Then imagine yourself setting up an alternative
telephone network infrastructure, 100 years after 1907 when Vail started
buying up (or stealing through state legislatures) all the little
independents to build a national network.

Be very grateful that we have multiple massive networks serving most
areas of Portland.  Even if they are all Evil Corporations, at least
they are somewhat competitive and there are slightly different flavors
of vanilla.  Back before cell phones and VOIP, you used the one telephone
monopoly, with bundled long distance, or you used ham radio, or you
sent letters.  Hell, in some parts of the world, there is still only one
ISP and one phone company, and they are both owned by the post office.

It is usually new technology the opens up new communication possibilities.
ISP has already happened, and is now a commodity.  Probably the next
opportunity is mesh, or 802.11N WiMax.  But once that starts serving
a lot of people, economies of scale will kick in again, and the little
guys will be too expensive compared to the big guys.  Again.  

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

grudgingly cut Qwest some slack since learning that their CEO had to 
suffer losing out on some federal gov't business by voicing his

From krisa at subtend.net  Fri Jan 11 13:36:09 2008
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Fri, 11 Jan 2008 13:36:09 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Thin-clients
In-Reply-To: <ca5165530801111228p26582003vfe173116538d42e3@mail.gmail.com>
References: <4787C5F5.8010109@subtend.net>	<ca5165530801111220h2bdb5447ndbca5e815800054b@mail.gmail.com>	<4787D0D3.8080404@subtend.net>

Message-ID: <4787E149.5030008@subtend.net>

Larry Brigman wrote:
> What is wrong with them.  Maybe we (PLUG member) can help you figure out how
> to get them working.  (lots of details are probably needed to
> troubleshoot this).

I would rather offer the PLUG community the opportunity to get paid to get them
working.  If anyone is interested contact me offline.  I have budget, and it
would really help out a local school.

The main issue is the 2nd DHCP attempt is failing.  It PXE boots off DHCP, loads
the kernel, and appears to fail around the IP-Config DHCP attempt (couldn't find
much info on the klibc ipconfig command..  ipconfig is not a typo for ifconfig).

I'm working off the 2.6.18 kernel (kernel.org) with the driver patches and
.config file provided by the manufacturer of the SoC:

http://www.dmp.com.tw/tech/Vortex86SX/

The default Edubuntu kernel for LTSP clients is 2.6.22, which I had to swap out
by chroot building a new kernel in the LTSP environment (/opt/ltsp/i386) and
ltsp-update-kernel.

On the packet level, I see DHCP Discover packets coming from the device, and the
server sending an Offer back.  These two packets loop forever, the device never
wanting to accept the Offer.

A System76 laptop (when switched back to the stock LTSP 2.6.22 kernel) boots
just fine and we get an X login window.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

I realize you probably don't have time to play and experiment, and I 
know that your switch-fu dwarfs mine, but I'm wondering...

Are these machines plugged into a switch or a hub? If the former, try 
putting a hub between the client and the switch. In some cases, I've 
seen that clear up the problem. My lingering suspicion is that the 
issue has been switch configuration, but I have no switch-fu to speak 
of, so I've always been stymied.

Also, can you assign these machines a static address? If so, do the 
network drivers work correctly?

> You're not confusing the speed test's Kb/s with your torrent client's 
> report of KB/s are you?  Because that'd be just under one tenth as big in

I just checked, and you are correct. I use Ktorrent, which reports
download speeds in KB/s, not Kb/s.

Having said that, often torrents download at only 5-10 KB/s. At the
moment I am downloading Gentoo amd64 and it is averaging about 500
KB/s. But it depends on lots of other factors. Even assuming that 500
KB/s is the same as 5000 Kb/s, the tests I used (suggested by Joe and
Mark previously) ranged from 7800 to 23,500 Kb/s, so I am still limited
by something other than the maximum Comcast is giving me. In fact, it
always seems to be something other than the Comcast limit.

I used EasyStreet as my ISP for many years - great support AND they 
don't hang up on you if you mention "linux" when you are communicating 
with their support team.  They do provide DSL static IP support through 
your phone carrier.  Seems to me that the cost was about $100/mo.  My 
phone carrier (Verizon) made me get a "business" account in order to get 
the static IP (after all, if you are not a business, why would you need 
one?)  I use dynamic DNS now, WAY cheaper.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Jan 12 09:19:20 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 12 Jan 2008 09:19:20 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Static IPs - was Re:  DSL in Portland
In-Reply-To: <811271.78442.qm@web51406.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

On Fri, Jan 11, 2008 at 09:02:42PM -0800, syang895 at yahoo.com wrote:
> 
> I'm pretty new to Oregon (actually down in Corvallis)
> but I'm trying to help a friend with his network.  Can
> anyone recommend an ISP that provides static IPs? 
> Unlike my old stomping grounds, Portland (and Oregon)
> has a surprising number of providers.  

Greetings, Sue!

Besides the suggestions already mentioned, perhaps the cheapest way
to get a static IP address (and the permission to use it) is to rent
an offsite virtual machine.  My outwards facing server is a Xen
instance at the Dallas TX Rimuhosting site.  For $31/mo I get a
virtual "small machine" with two static IP addresses in a facility
with 10gbps connectivity.  They offer $20/mo plans with 1 IP and 
less memory.  I drive a virtual private network from my home network
on Comcast.

I can use dyndns.org for dynamic DNS to my comcast home network, but
there will be downtime periods when they change my IP address, and if
I am running an inbound server I am violating the contract I agreed
to.  As others note, if I run a server then Comcast wants to sell a
business-class connection for double the price.

For some interactive, large-disk-usage applications, like serving or
torrenting giant files, then I would have to back the smallish disk
space that Rimuhosting offers with extra space at this end of the
pipe, and that slows things way down.  So you may not be able to use
a virtual for some applications.

Still, virtuals get around many of the restrictions that local ISPs
may have.  Since the local ISP only "sees" my VPN uplink, and I can
(if necessary) run that to port 80 on the virtual and even use
steganography to shape it like "approved" network traffic, I can 
bypass most practical restrictions.  

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

On Fri, Jan 11, 2008 at 04:23:39PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:

> I just checked, and you are correct. I use Ktorrent, which reports
> download speeds in KB/s, not Kb/s.
> 
> Having said that, often torrents download at only 5-10 KB/s. At the
> moment I am downloading Gentoo amd64 and it is averaging about 500
> KB/s. But it depends on lots of other factors. Even assuming that 500
> KB/s is the same as 5000 Kb/s, the tests I used (suggested by Joe and
> Mark previously) ranged from 7800 to 23,500 Kb/s, so I am still limited
> by something other than the maximum Comcast is giving me. In fact, it
> always seems to be something other than the Comcast limit.

Torrent is as slow as the slowest machine out there acting as a server
to you.  There may be some folk with modems, and most broadband services
(cable and DSL) have much slower upload (downloading to you) then
download (uploading to you).  Adding in handshaking (to those slow
sites) and it gets even worse - you are waiting for the snail-slow 
handshake packets to leave their machines.

So you may have very fast download, from a real server on a fast link,
but unless you are communicating only with high-speed-bidirectional
peers you will see some slow behavior from torrent.  You are on Comcast,
with 6Mbit download and 384Kbit upload maximums.  On a "fair" torrent,
you will be uploading as much as you are downloading (slowing down both
directions), and you will be losing a lot of speed to handshaking and
and overhead.  So, you should expect to see a maximum of 20KByte/sec
behavior from torrent;  any more, and you are taking more than you give.

Well, you share in other ways, but bittorrent doesn't know that ... :-)

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

On Jan 11, 2008 1:36 PM, Kris <krisa at subtend.net> wrote:
> Larry Brigman wrote:
> The main issue is the 2nd DHCP attempt is failing.  It PXE boots off DHCP, loads
> the kernel, and appears to fail around the IP-Config DHCP attempt (couldn't find
> much info on the klibc ipconfig command..  ipconfig is not a typo for ifconfig).
>
> I'm working off the 2.6.18 kernel (kernel.org) with the driver patches and
> .config file provided by the manufacturer of the SoC:
>
> http://www.dmp.com.tw/tech/Vortex86SX/

If  I understand you the second time DHCP runs (from the OS after PXE
booting) it fails.

I've seen an issue like this before.  The network switch is a cisco
and does not have portfast enabled.  The issue being when the network
driver is loaded by the OS it drops the link and brings it back up
which causes the switch to attempt to rebuild the spanning tree which
can take long enough for dhcp to fail.

In summary: Does 8Mbits Down translate to 8KBytes Down, or 1000KBytes Down?*

So i was on the phone with a Comcast rep today, and I asked him what my down
speed would be, since I just ordered service to be installed on Weds. He
said "8 Megs". So I asked him "8 Mega*bits*?*". *And he said "yes, 8 Mega*
bits*".

I'm not an expert, but in my head I thought that 8 Megabits = 1000KiloBytes
(since (8Mbits / 8) / 1000 = 1000). So I asked him if that meant I would
would be getting 1000KBytes down, and he said no. (I like to think of things
in Bytes not Bits. It's not like I'm going to get a new iPod and say "Dude,
check out my new 5,100,000,000 bits iPod!") He said that would mean I was
getting 8000KBytes down. I couldn't see why, so I said, "are you sure that
means I would be getting 8000KBytes down, and NOT 1000KBytes down?" and he
said yes, he was sure.

The rep then started asking me why I thought I would only be getting
1000KBytes down, "is it because you are going to share your internet with 8
other people?"

You nailed it very well Keith. Depending on how many peers you can connect
to and which machines have the pieces of the torrent you currently need.
I've had really good speeds and then see them drop to near nothing only to
pick back up minutes later after I had received whatever piece I needed that
happened to be sent to me via a slower peer. Comcast's traffic shaping (if
they are still doing it, and I have some non-scientific data from my torrent
use that suggests they are) still really sucks, but as I think has been
mentioned before, it is their network and they are allowed to do whatever
they please. I do find it odd that it doesn't appear that they are shaping
Gnutella traffic.

Drew-

	<e2aeccd60801122040y5d008d9u5edd5116b2f412e8@mail.gmail.com>

On Sat, 12 Jan 2008 20:40:56 -0800
"drew wymore" <drew.wymore at gmail.com> dijo:

> <snipped>

> > > I just checked, and you are correct. I use Ktorrent, which reports
> > > download speeds in KB/s, not Kb/s.
> > >
> > > Having said that, often torrents download at only 5-10 KB/s. At the
> > > moment I am downloading Gentoo amd64 and it is averaging about 500
> > > KB/s. But it depends on lots of other factors. Even assuming that 500
> > > KB/s is the same as 5000 Kb/s, the tests I used (suggested by Joe and
> > > Mark previously) ranged from 7800 to 23,500 Kb/s, so I am still limited
> > > by something other than the maximum Comcast is giving me. In fact, it
> > > always seems to be something other than the Comcast limit.
> >
> > Torrent is as slow as the slowest machine out there acting as a server
> > to you.  There may be some folk with modems, and most broadband services
> > (cable and DSL) have much slower upload (downloading to you) then
> > download (uploading to you).  Adding in handshaking (to those slow
> > sites) and it gets even worse - you are waiting for the snail-slow
> > handshake packets to leave their machines.
> >
> > So you may have very fast download, from a real server on a fast link,
> > but unless you are communicating only with high-speed-bidirectional
> > peers you will see some slow behavior from torrent.  You are on Comcast,
> > with 6Mbit download and 384Kbit upload maximums.  On a "fair" torrent,
> > you will be uploading as much as you are downloading (slowing down both
> > directions), and you will be losing a lot of speed to handshaking and
> > and overhead.  So, you should expect to see a maximum of 20KByte/sec
> > behavior from torrent;  any more, and you are taking more than you give.

> You nailed it very well Keith. Depending on how many peers you can connect
> to and which machines have the pieces of the torrent you currently need.
> I've had really good speeds and then see them drop to near nothing only to
> pick back up minutes later after I had received whatever piece I needed that
> happened to be sent to me via a slower peer. Comcast's traffic shaping (if
> they are still doing it, and I have some non-scientific data from my torrent
> use that suggests they are) still really sucks, but as I think has been
> mentioned before, it is their network and they are allowed to do whatever
> they please. I do find it odd that it doesn't appear that they are shaping
> Gnutella traffic.

The other day while downloading the Gentoo DVD ISO it started out at
750-850 KB/s. By the time it ended the speed was down to 350-450 KB/s,
and Ktorrent told me the final average was about 500 KB/s. Throughout
the download time I would look at Ktorrent from time to time and the
same seeders were present all the time - that is, all seeders had 100%,
all but three were choked, and there were no leechers besides me. The
same three non-choked were always seeding me. The total speed might
drop, but the percentage of the total that each seeder gave me was
roughly constant. For example, if the total amount was 500 KB/s, one
seeder in Germany was giving me roughly 325 KB/s, one in Brazil was
giving me about 150 KB/s, and one in the US was giving me around 25
KB/s. When the total was 700 KB/s the same seeders gave me the same
ratios of the total.

I recall downloading Gutsy on the day it was released. There were
hundreds of seeders and hundreds of leechers. Yet I never saw a speed
in excess of 100 KB/s, in spite of the fact that there were dozens of
non-choked seeders with 100%, many of whom were sending me nothing. 

The problem is that there is no way for me to tell what is actually
happening. From the above it makes me want to think that Comcast senses
when I am using a torrent and "shapes" my download so I am slowly
throttled back during the download. But am I being paranoid to think
that? I don't know and, absent information from a snitch inside
Comcast, I will probably never know. Torrents work in very mysterious
ways. 

I can add that during the Gentoo ISO download I also installed a couple
dozen upgrades for my desktop computer with Download Manager, some of
which were multiple MB. Those downloaded at 500-600 KB/s and I watched
the Gentoo download speed as the upgrades downloaded. The Gentoo
download was unaffected. In other words, I had additional download
capacity throughout the Gentoo download. If the three that were seeding
me were not choked, why did they not send at a faster rate? Because
Comcast was "shaping" my download? Because Ktorrent reports them as not
choked when they really are? Very mysterious things, these torrents.

Yes and no. They have an obligation to protect themselves from customers 
who impact other customers and from customers who violate laws as well. 
They have an obligation to adhere to generally accepted accounting 
principles, etc. In other words, it's not as simple as "The customer is 
always right" any more.

That said, when I first got Comcast and started doing Linux CD/DVD 
downloads using Azureus, it seems to me that I usually got max download 
speeds unless the seed population was too sparse to supply what I 
needed. I forget how long it's been -- three years, maybe. I haven't 
seen such download speeds recently, so either there is more demand for 
bandwidth than there is supply or something else has changed.

That also said, they blocked port 25 on me last weekend without telling 
me. I had to queue up in their chat line to find that out and get a 
workaround from them. All they would tell me was that there was 
excessive email coming from my account. It may have been excessive 
*bytes* -- sometimes I send decent-sized (4 MB) PDFs and that may have 
triggered their alert mechanism.

I can second the recommendation for SpiritOne, having signed with them
last year after hearing about them on this group.  But they don't
provide DLS services in Corvallis (where I live), so I use them only
as an ISP (hosting eehouse.org).  I used Qwest for DSL until a couple
of months ago, then switched to Comcast when Qwest confirmed that only
their slowest speed service was available to my house and work
requirements made that inadequate.

The Comcast installer (who, FWIW, was cool with my no Windoze in
sight) said my IP address would never change.  He was right for the
about a month.  But I use ez-ipupdate and zoneedit to keep my IP
addresses available.  And for the occasional time that doesn't work
have hourly cron jobs that record IP addresses out where I can get
them.  Works for me....

On Sun, 13 Jan 2008, Mike Connors wrote:
> Denis Heidtmann wrote:
>> drew wymore wrote:
>>> it is their network and they are allowed to do whatever they please.
>> Do they have an obligation to tell customers what they are doing?
>>
> Technically, only in as much as what is defined in the terms of service
> agreement. Which I don't think too many people have read in full nor
> declined using their service based on it except a few enlightened people
> on this mailing list.

I think that Denis is hinting at a moral and ethical obligation which goes 
far and away beyond any merely legal entanglements.

After all, the law is there only to atttempt to reconcile design flaws. 
Doing what is legal is doing the bare minimum and I would argue that we 
have an obligation to do much more than that.  The source of that 
obligation is the enormous dependence we have on our society and culture; 
for the technology we were given free of charge by the billions of people 
who came before us -- from the computers we use to the language we speak 
-- and for the culture itself and all of the art and literature that gave 
us this rich, fertile soil upon which we build our lives and in which we 
grow our own ideas.

The legal obligation is what you cite when someone is being egregiously 
antisocial, but it is not our sole obligation to society.

> The problem I have with many ISPs is that they set invisible limits, 
> don't tell you when and why they're employing traffic control 
> mechanisms, and they don't provide any accounting of your bandwidth 
> usage.

I agree that it is unethical to provide a network that is not transparent 
-- that changes the form or content of the data or the shape of the 
bandwidth that crosses it.

I don't understand what benefit you think you would receive from your ISP 
tracking your bandwidth usage.  Surely this is something better done by 
the users themselves.  It is, after all, personal information that is 
maybe not such a good thing to collect in a big database outside the 
control of the people it concerns.

J.

On Sun, 13 Jan 2008, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:
> Denis Heidtmann wrote:
>> drew wymore wrote:
>>> it is their network and they are allowed to do whatever they please.
>>
>> Do they have an obligation to tell customers what they are doing?
>
> Yes and no. They have an obligation to protect themselves from customers
> who impact other customers and from customers who violate laws as well.

Actually, they have no obligation whatsoever to protect themselves from 
customers who violate the law -- at least as not insofar as those 
purportedly illegal actions are not service effecting.

First, you must remember that whether or not an action is against the law 
is solely the determination of the judicial system.  It is not for Comcast 
or any other private entity to decide whether or not the actions of anyone 
but themselves are in violation of the law.

Second, network carriers are not responsible for any illegal actions 
carried out by their customers.  As a common carrier, they are shielded 
from such legal responsibility.  Should a customer be suspected by some 
legal authority to be breaking the law, there are channels and regulations 
that define explicitly how the law enforcement agencies can and should (if 
their interest is, indeed, upholding the law) interface with the ISP to 
take appropriate, legal action.  Anything beyond that legal proscription 
is a violation of the rights of the users and of the ISP to go about their 
lives unmolested.

> They have an obligation to adhere to generally accepted accounting 
> principles, etc. In other words, it's not as simple as "The customer is 
> always right" any more.

As I wrote in my previous post, their obligation goes far beyond the 
legal.  And going back to Denis' question, their obligation to inform 
customers has nothing at all to do with any of these legal obligations.

What, if anything, frees them from the ethical responsibility of sharing 
their policies and practices on the handling of customer data with their 
customers?

J.

> I don't understand what benefit you think you would receive from your ISP 
> tracking your bandwidth usage.  Surely this is something better done by 
> the users themselves.  It is, after all, personal information that is 
> maybe not such a good thing to collect in a big database outside the 
> control of the people it concerns.
>
> J.
>

Any business internet account would get full bandwidth accounting 
complete with bar graphs, pie charts, and threshold alerts for 
over-usage. The point of it is to see how much actual bandwidth you're 
consuming. I would imagine the average 40hr/wk working person is on the 
internet from their home approximately 20 hrs/wk. And for that same 
average person a very small percentage of that time is for 
high-bandwidth usage.

If I'm hogging bandwidth and making internet access in my neighborhood 
miserable for everyone else than I should pay more. You have to 
understand how these companies dole out bandwidth. The industry standard 
is to over-subscribe 4 to 1. They are just trying to get as many people 
signed up for access and are banking on the fact that *most* people only 
use their connection a few hrs/day and *generally* don't consume large 
amounts of bandwidth for long periods of time.

--mike

Oh boy, another stale subject line to retarget.  I may have found my
purpose in life!

On Sun, Jan 13, 2008 at 03:42:46PM -0800, Mike Connors wrote:
> Any business internet account would get full bandwidth accounting 
> complete with bar graphs, pie charts, and threshold alerts for 
> over-usage. The point of it is to see how much actual bandwidth you're 
> consuming. I would imagine the average 40hr/wk working person is on the 
> internet from their home approximately 20 hrs/wk. And for that same 
> average person a very small percentage of that time is for 
> high-bandwidth usage.
> 
> If I'm hogging bandwidth and making internet access in my neighborhood 
> miserable for everyone else than I should pay more. You have to 
> understand how these companies dole out bandwidth. The industry standard 
> is to over-subscribe 4 to 1. They are just trying to get as many people 
> signed up for access and are banking on the fact that *most* people only 
> use their connection a few hrs/day and *generally* don't consume large 
> amounts of bandwidth for long periods of time.

Agree with most, though I suspect the "average" person (besides having
one mammary, one testicle, and being mostly dead) is on the 'net far
less than 20 hours per week.  The average person is also not using the
telephones, the roads, or the sewers continuously either, which are all
systems that work only because of the averaging of large numbers of
random and low-usage customers.

In the early nineties, I designed a line of chips called "non-blocking
crossbar switches" for a company called I-Cube Design Systems.  They
were originally intended for hardware logic simulation, but I-Cube's
original simulator customer ended up in legal battles over patents.
I-Cube found themselves a new customer that could use switches, a small
Bay area outfit called Cisco Systems.  The 'net took off, Cisco took
off, and I-Cube had by far the best switch chips at the time.

Most companies provided switches that were small and complicated and
slow.  They tended to use something called "Banyan Trees" based on the
idea that the average amount of traffic needed fewer switches than a
complete "any to any" matrix.  That made the switches "blocking", that
is, an established route could prevent another from taking place.  This
only occured in periods of high usage.  However, usage of any 
oversubscribed resource is usually "fractal", meaning that there are
periods of very high usage that don't conform to bell curve statistics.
For land-line telephones, it is Mothers Day.  For sewers, it is the 
Superbowl station breaks.  And for the internet, it is news events and
(locally) slashdotting.  Banyan tree networks crumble under the load,
way-overbuilt non-blocking switches do not.

The I-Cube switches were any-to-any and non-blocking - you could light
up the entire switch without blocking messages.  They were also easy to
test, cheap, low latency, and wicked fast.  I-Cube was growing 30% a
quarter, with 50% profit on sales, when the V.C.s decided that they
couldn't explain to IPO investors what the company did, and sent I-Cube
off chasing negative-profit easy-to-explain stuff like Ethernet chipsets.  

The next big shakeup in the net will be the next big event that sends
the zillions of "average" users to camp out online, looking at videos
they can't get from the network.  An oversubscribed network may fail,
angering the "occasional" customers and making them easy pickings for
competitors after the Big Event is over.  

I suspect a lot of customers have 'net connections so it will be there
in an emergency, and are not big everyday users.  Whichever network is
best "shaped" for extreme usage periods will pick up a lot of customers
after the next crisis.  As the cost of provisioning bandwidth continues
to plummet, extra bandwidth, and the ability to allocate that bandwidth
to the maximum number of "average" customers, is what forward-thinking
ISPs should be looking for.   A good proxy for that might actually be
open-source downloads and bittorrent - as long as you can cut throttle
those down when the fecal matter hits the air circulator.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

From rsteff at comcast.net  Tue Jan 15 07:50:35 2008
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Tue, 15 Jan 2008 07:50:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Where'd sg0 go?
Message-ID: <478CD64B.3060506@comcast.net>

At one point yesterday afternoon the wind was picking up enough to cause
some beeping from my battery backup. Since it appeared that it was just
getting started and was getting worse, I shut down my various computers.
As it turned out, that was the worst of the wind, and we never did lose
power out here.

I have a post-it note that reminds me to change the permissions of
/dev/sg0 to 666 so I can use my scanner without running as root. When I
tried to do that I found that there is no /dev/sg0. I realized that when
I shut down the computer yesterday I also shut off the scanner. I didn't
power up the scanner this morning. I remember that the instructions for
the scanner (when I used to use it on Win98) said that the scanner had
to be powered on before booting or the machine would not be able to find
it.

Is that true of all SCSI devices? I.e., does the hardware have to be
powered up for the system to see before /dev/sg0 will be created?

Is there some way to tell the system to expect a SCSI device sometime in
the future without having it powered up before booting?

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens



From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Jan 15 08:00:40 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 15 Jan 2008 08:00:40 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Where'd sg0 go?
In-Reply-To: <478CD64B.3060506@comcast.net>
References: <478CD64B.3060506@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.00.0801150753010.5404@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Tue, 15 Jan 2008, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> At one point yesterday afternoon the wind was picking up enough to 
> cause some beeping from my battery backup. Since it appeared that it 
> was just getting started and was getting worse, I shut down my 
> various computers. As it turned out, that was the worst of the wind, 
> and we never did lose power out here.
>
> I have a post-it note that reminds me to change the permissions of 
> /dev/sg0 to 666 so I can use my scanner without running as root. 
> When I tried to do that I found that there is no /dev/sg0. I 
> realized that when I shut down the computer yesterday I also shut 
> off the scanner. I didn't power up the scanner this morning. I 
> remember that the instructions for the scanner (when I used to use 
> it on Win98) said that the scanner had to be powered on before 
> booting or the machine would not be able to find it.
>
> Is that true of all SCSI devices? I.e., does the hardware have to be 
> powered up for the system to see before /dev/sg0 will be created?
>
> Is there some way to tell the system to expect a SCSI device 
> sometime in the future without having it powered up before booting?

Some SCSI hardware does great with hotplugging, but it's not 
universally true.

I think SANE ships with a hotplug script that can be used to identify 
recently arrived hardware, but I don't know if it works with SCSI 
devices.

Various Linux distributions include utilities for rescanning a SCSI 
bus. Google for "$DISTRO rescan scsi bus" for your flavor. Under 
RHEL/CentOS, for instance, the directions (which might also work with 
your distribution) were easy to find:

   http://misterd77.blogspot.com/2007_12_01_archive.html

If all else fails, you can "modprobe -r" your SCSI driver (assuming 
your boot device doesn't use it!), power on your scanner, then reload 
the module(s). Your bus will get a clean scan and sg0 should pop up in 
dmesg.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Tue Jan 15 09:57:53 2008
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Tue, 15 Jan 2008 09:57:53 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Where'd sg0 go?
In-Reply-To: <478CD64B.3060506@comcast.net>

Message-ID: <ca5165530801150957g1b1a3d51p9809aeb9dfb77219@mail.gmail.com>

On Jan 15, 2008 7:50 AM, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
> At one point yesterday afternoon the wind was picking up enough to cause
> some beeping from my battery backup. Since it appeared that it was just
> getting started and was getting worse, I shut down my various computers.
> As it turned out, that was the worst of the wind, and we never did lose
> power out here.
>
> I have a post-it note that reminds me to change the permissions of
> /dev/sg0 to 666 so I can use my scanner without running as root. When I
> tried to do that I found that there is no /dev/sg0. I realized that when
> I shut down the computer yesterday I also shut off the scanner. I didn't
> power up the scanner this morning. I remember that the instructions for
> the scanner (when I used to use it on Win98) said that the scanner had
> to be powered on before booting or the machine would not be able to find
> it.
>
> Is that true of all SCSI devices? I.e., does the hardware have to be
> powered up for the system to see before /dev/sg0 will be created?
>
> Is there some way to tell the system to expect a SCSI device sometime in
> the future without having it powered up before booting?
>

In the SG utils package there is a script to scan the SCSI bus.  This
will allow you to
see and access devices that were powered up after the bus was scanned.
Actually the devices do show up when you look at the procfs but
getting the device
added requires manual intervention.

Becasue most of the SG driver is now standard in the kernel the script
is in various places
on the net.  My Google search resulted in this one.
http://www.garloff.de/kurt/linux/rescan-scsi-bus.sh-1.25

Adapt it as needed.  The command that is needed is scsi add-device as follows:
        echo "scsi add-single-device $devnr" > /proc/scsi/scsi

From alan.olsen at gmail.com  Wed Jan 16 09:43:55 2008
From: alan.olsen at gmail.com (Alan Olsen)
Date: Wed, 16 Jan 2008 09:43:55 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Metrofi does not connect (was Rogue of the week)
In-Reply-To: <20080110181740.GA2209@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <47854048.80100@seitzassoc.com>
	<15469.198.182.194.170.1199916407.squirrel@clueserver.org>
	<20080110181740.GA2209@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <55cf303a0801160943x2a0e9e57u5086f274ed95596b@mail.gmail.com>

On Jan 10, 2008 10:17 AM, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:

> On Wed, Jan 09, 2008 at 02:06:47PM -0800, Alan wrote:
> > > Chief rabble-rouser Russell puts Metrofi in the spotlight.
> > >    http://wweek.com/editorial/3409/10212/
> >
> > My laptop can see a MetroFi node from my desk at work.  I have never
> been
> > able to get an IP address from it or connect in any usable way.
> >
> > Amazingly bogus crap.
>
> What MetroFi promises to do - blanket a city, outdoors and by implication
> indoors, with 802.11B wireless access - is probably technically
> impossible.  You would probably need as many access points as there
> are light bulbs in the city now, for similar reasons.
>
> As far as "you can see them, but you can't connect", though, the
> reasons are similar to the light bulb analogy.  You can see a street
> light from a long way away, but it is a lot less likely that someone
> at the street light can see *you* from a long way away.  Each Metrofi
> radio is greater than 100mW (200mW? 500mW?) and only transmits into a
> flattened cone of space, while typical built-in Wifi is a meager 5mW
> and spews energy in all directions.  So your laptop may be effectively
> invisible to any access point at an appreciable distance (say, farther
> away than 10 feet, through walls, trees, or above or below the cone).
> They deliver more radio power to you than you do to them.
>
> You may have more luck with a PCMCIA wireless card (some PRISM2 cards
> are 200mW), if you can find one with a proper chipset and Linux drivers.
> Or maybe not, no cards are as powerful and directed as the Metrofi
> antennas.  Adding a large dish antenna to the card and pointing it at
> the Metrofi antenna might be enough.  That may violate some FCC rules.
>
> Connection is not nearly as much a problem with a typical PTP node (the
> access points are indoors in specific places, mostly, in the same room
> as you are), or with a Meraki mesh (zillions of cheap access points, if
> one can't see you, others probably can).  But those are limited area
> coverage, hundreds of square feet rather than hundreds of square miles.
>

Actually the problem may not be the wireless card at all.  DHCP appears to
be pretty screwed up on the latest Fedora 8.  They just pushed out and
update to that and some other network apps.  Hopefully that fixed the
problem.

The card itself works very well.  It has much better reception than the
Athros card or the Prism2 card.  Kistmet shows a huge number of networks.  I
just can't connect to them very well.

If I have to i will revert back to Fedora 7.  It will be a pain though
because there are things in F8 I really need and don't have the time to
build for F7.

From alan at clueserver.org  Thu Jan 17 20:33:18 2008
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Thu, 17 Jan 2008 20:33:18 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ANNOUNCE: Advanced Topics Jan 21, 2008
Message-ID: <1200630798.14767.7.camel@kratos.fnordora.org>

PLUG Advanced Topics meeting
January 21st 2008
7pm - 9pm

Location:  Jax
           826 SW 2nd Avenue 
           Portland, Oregon

Speaker:  Russell Senior
Topic:    Troubleshooting Wireless Networks

 "I will give a brief description of linux tools useful in 
 diagnosing wifi problems: iwconfig, dhclient, kismet and the like.

 Driver problems are beyond the scope of this talk.  To me, driver
 problems boil down to (a) picking the right hardware or (b) being
 patient and waiting (possibly forever).  At Personal Telco, we focus
 on building infrastructure.  Using that infrastructure is a client
 problem.  We are happy to share what we know, but given (a) we
 already picked the right hardware so we might not have that much to
 share.

 Bearing that caveat in mind, I can try to address specific problems
 raised by the audience.

 I can also talk about my recent MetroFi survey, if people are
 interested."

Qouted from today's Slashdot Stories:

Time Warner Cable to Test Tiered Bandwidth Caps  

"The plan is to offer various service tiers with bandwidth fees
for overuse, as well as a bandwidth meter customers can use to help them
stay within their allotment. If it works out, they will consider a
nation-wide rollout. Interestingly, the memo also claims that 5% of
subscribers use over 50% of the total network bandwidth."

Read full story: http://arstechnica.com/news.ars/post/20080116-leaked-memo-time-warner-cable-to-trial-hard-bandwidth-caps.html

From brent at servuhome.net  Fri Jan 18 14:21:14 2008
From: brent at servuhome.net (Brent Jones)
Date: Fri, 18 Jan 2008 14:21:14 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Monitoring Unix systems with open source
Message-ID: <ee9f3b480801181421l689b2803kc8ade30abe7f1129@mail.gmail.com>

Hello PLUG,
I don't post often, but I thought I would share my experience lately with a
few tools I have been using in a mix FreeBSD and Windows environment.

Cacti:
Open source SNMP monitoring tool that collects various data from hosts and
graphs performance and availability trends. Useful for capacity planning,
network switch and router performance, and graphing anything really that has
a string or calculable delta.
http://www.cacti.net

Zabbix:
Open source SNMP and Agent-based monitoring tool that performs similar
functions as Cacti, but uses installable agents to get more information
about a host system, such as running processes, database monitoring, system
health (raid status, power supplies, etc).
http://www.zabbix.com

SNMPTT:
Collects SNMP traps, and translates them into meaningful human readable
content. Useful for translating vendor or enterprise specific SNMP trap
messages and various OIDs. Can send those traps into a database, to an
external program, or as SMS messages and e-mails.
http://www.snmptt.org/

In my environment, we are monitoring a broad mix of FreeBSD, Windows, and
various networking equipment. Using the above tools give us superb
visualization and feedback on the performance and availability of our
systems.
I've always been meaning to write somewhat of a guide to get these setup,
but the individual websites do a much better job of that than I could.

I hope these links help others who have been looking for production quality
open source monitoring programs!

-- 
Brent Jones
brent at servuhome.net

Just this week I migrated from a Nagios/Cacti setup to a Zabbix one.
I've been using nagios for years... but zabbix quickly gained my favor
for ease of configuration, speed, and "triggers".

You do not have to use agents to watch network services like IMAP,
DNS, etc. You can also use SNMP instead of the agent. It has pretty
good jabber support as long as you include it during the ./configure.

I highly suggest it!

From brent at servuhome.net  Fri Jan 18 15:28:06 2008
From: brent at servuhome.net (Brent Jones)
Date: Fri, 18 Jan 2008 15:28:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Monitoring Unix systems with open source

Message-ID: <ee9f3b480801181528v59e2e064l7d8828c32ebc3f73@mail.gmail.com>

> Zenoss is another application, but it didn't look as baked as Zabbix
> when I reviewed them a few months ago.



I used the VMware edition of Zenoss while trying out various systems, and
was not impressed.
They are trying to merge all of the functionlity of Cacti, Nagios, and
inventory functions into one package -- admirable effort, but it doesn't
feel "as one" still.
And the UI is not intuitive, left me second guessing where I should be
looking for information -- not what I want to do when I want to get
something quickly.
Their commercial version may be better, I did not do a product demo of that
yet (and their prices make me inclined not to look at it either).

Zabbix, Cacti, and SNMPTT fill 100% of our needs, with minimal hardware and
training overhead.

If anyone used BigBrother/BigSister, then I would suggest Zabbix, as you
will be familiar with the concept of "traps", for handling events.
Zabbix also has a built in SNMP trap receiver, but I have not attempted to
use it because the documentation is sparse and doesnt seem to do all that
SNMPTT can do.

Brent Jones
brent at servuhome.net


From eli.stair at gmail.com  Fri Jan 18 15:55:51 2008
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Fri, 18 Jan 2008 15:55:51 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Monitoring Unix systems with open source
In-Reply-To: <47912ECA.1030901@subtend.net>
References: <ee9f3b480801181421l689b2803kc8ade30abe7f1129@mail.gmail.com>
	<47912ECA.1030901@subtend.net>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0801181555k59784046h40f17ac9eaae54ab@mail.gmail.com>

I just spent about 4 months doing some real hard testing, evaluating
(testing in production, writing tools to manage, integrating with
other apps) Zabbix/Zenoss/Hyperic/someothers to replace a
Nagios/Ganglia/Cacti system I've built over the last few years.  IMO
in summary, if you want to do anything more complex/custom than the
out-of-the-box features, Zabbix is the best bet (and is period).
Hyperic is closed-source in part and isn't very flexible, Zenoss is
great in theory, but changes require __LOTS__ of coding for the
middleware (Zope), and most of the features really are half-baked, and
most bugs were slow to get resolved (if ever).

Zabbix is the most extendable in a variety of ways; while I dislike
writing PHP, it is utterly simple, extensible, and fairly
flexible/capable (and only runs the frontend), support for SQL
backends is great and good for integration with external tools.
Zabbix flat out kicks ass as far as extensibility (able to arbitrarily
add SNMP/agent/script -based collection), custom collection methods
via any scripts on any platform (even Windows!).  Only problem is in
really large setups (thousands of monitored systems) which most people
probably won't run into... while I've spent over a month
isolating/fixing bugs with the Zabbix developers, its' still insanely
resource-intensive.  They HAVE been unbelievably responsive in fixing
each and every bug or performance/scalability issue I've brought up.

Even better, zabbix isn't a crippleware/restricted/dual-licensed scam
to get you hooked: the software is fully-featured, all GPL2.  The
support is CHEAP, at least several tens of thousands of dollars per
year cheaper than the nearest competitor in my situation.

Caveat emptor:

While I can monitor the same number of hosts (though fewer metrics,
less often) with RRD-based systems on a single quad-proc/8GB machine,
Zabbix is now requiring (with ~2000 hosts, ~100,000 values, avg
5-minute resolution) two dedicated 8-way MySQL servers, a 32G SSD and
4TB of FC-SAN.  It doesn't scale the same as RRD's with their
automatic reduction in resolution over time and simple pre-sized
binary format.  It DOES give you the ability to keep as
high-resolution data as long as you want, and amazingly it allows for
actually doing this, which nothing else has even allowed to this
degree of precision and granularity.

Cheers,

/eli

> visualization and feedback on the performance and availability of our
> systems.

As someone who's still very new to open source monitoring tools, I have 
to cast my vote for Munin. Quick and easy to setup, plug-ins are a dead 
simple to write with any scripting language. Also, 1 advantage I feel 
Munin has over other SNMP based systems is that it uses collects data 
info via sysstat, (a collection Linux performance tools such as iostat, 
sar, etc.) I feel this provides greater data accuracy and a deeper level 
of insight into your servers.

I introduced Munin at my last job with a small internet startup and the 
developers just loved it and wrote all kinds of plug-ins to get a better 
understanding of how the system they wrote was functioning.

--mike

   Now and then I see an entry in the sshd log that looks like this one:

**Unmatched Entries**

   Since /etc/hosts.allow -- particularly line 8 -- has neither that IP

From teknotus at gmail.com  Sun Jan 20 08:33:58 2008
From: teknotus at gmail.com (Daniel Johnson)
Date: Sun, 20 Jan 2008 08:33:58 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Please explain SSHD log entry
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0801200812210.26837@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0801200812210.26837@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <ec0b5e080801200833p420c693ej1ed56963fea91dd3@mail.gmail.com>

On Jan 20, 2008 8:15 AM, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    Now and then I see an entry in the sshd log that looks like this one:
>
> **Unmatched Entries**
> warning: /etc/hosts.allow, line 8: host name/address mismatch: 67.138.24.31 != ns1.gloeblinks.com
>
>    Since /etc/hosts.allow -- particularly line 8 -- has neither that IP
> address (belongs to Electric Lightwave) nor that domain name, why do I get
> this warning? The only explanation that occurs to me is that the system
> recognized an unauthorized cracking attempt and prevented it, but did not
> have a better warning message to offer. Is that all it is?

Well they don't match in DNS.
67.138.24.31 looks up to be ns1.gloeblinks.com but
ns1.gloeblinks.com looks up to be 67.138.24.30

ssh sees that as possibly trying to spoof an address via DNS.  SSH is
justifiably paranoid.  Get DNS fixed so that forward, and reverse
lookups match, and the error should go away.

-- 
teknotus
Take Notice

>
> **Unmatched Entries**
> warning: /etc/hosts.allow, line 8: host name/address mismatch: 67.138.24.31 != ns1.gloeblinks.com
>
>    Since /etc/hosts.allow -- particularly line 8 -- has neither that IP
> address (belongs to Electric Lightwave) nor that domain name, why do I get
> this warning? The only explanation that occurs to me is that the system
> recognized an unauthorized cracking attempt and prevented it, but did not
> have a better warning message to offer. Is that all it is?

Well they don't match in DNS.
67.138.24.31 looks up to be ns1.gloeblinks.com but
ns1.gloeblinks.com looks up to be 67.138.24.30

ssh sees that as possibly trying to spoof an address via DNS.  SSH is
justifiably paranoid.  Get DNS fixed so that forward, and reverse
lookups match, and the error should go away.

-- 
teknotus
Take Notice

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Sun Jan 20 11:16:30 2008
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Sun, 20 Jan 2008 11:16:30 -0800
