John Jason Jordan wrote:
[snip]

> Having difference versions of Java installed always struck me as weird, although I have done so for a long time without problems. But one question is, how do I specify on the command line that it run on 1.5?

If you have 1.5 installed, I think you'll need to set your paths and 
environment variables so everything is where it is expected to be. On 
Gentoo Linux, there's a "java-config" that does all of this for you:

# java-config -L
The following VMs are available for generation-2:
1)	Sun 32bit JRE 1.6.0.03 [emul-linux-x86-java-1.6]
*)	Sun JDK 1.6.0.04 [sun-jdk-1.6]

If I had more versions, they would show up in the listing and I could do 
"java-config -S" to select it.

I can't help you with other versions of Linux. As a side note, I left 
Debian for Gentoo mostly because Debian had this religious attitude 
against Java and Gentoo supported it. If you're not on Gentoo, you can 
probably get a quick answer on the forum for your distro.

> >> If I use backticks, it seems to use the quotes as literals.  (So it
> tried
> >> to send '"site' to the server and process the first option as the
> >> hostname.)
> >>
> >> If I use backslashes to escape the spaces, it does the same thing.  It
> >> works on the command line, but in backticks it takes everything
> literally.
> >>
> >> Any ideas what I am doing wrong.
> >
> > Instead of backticks, try enclosing the command in "$()", ie:
> >
> >    $(ncftp -W "site var1=1, var2=2 var3=3" host filename /path/)
> >
> > No guarantees. I don't have access to genuine ksh at the moment.
> >
> > Terry
> > _______________________________________________
> > PLUG mailing list
> > PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> > http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
> >
>
> In addition, the NcFTP package comes with some utilities that might be
> better suited for shell scripting, namely "ncftpget" and "ncftpput".
> These have a command-line syntax, so you can easily substitute names, etc.
>

I have tried both of these.  Does not help.

Something in Korn is making the exec act wonky on strings with quotes in
them.  It ignores the quotes and just splits on spaces.  If you use the same
command on the shell prompt, it works fine.

I am working on getting them to let me rewrite the whole damn thing in
Perl.  Korn is just too damn inconstant.

Maybe I should see if the same problem exists in Bash.

From mconnors1 at gmail.com  Tue Jan 22 15:59:21 2008
From: mconnors1 at gmail.com (Mike Connors)
Date: Tue, 22 Jan 2008 15:59:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Installing Kubuntu in VMware on XP
In-Reply-To: <47967BC0.6030907@comcast.net>
References: <47967BC0.6030907@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <ebdfd14b0801221559g32aaf9ebq7b29fe35decf4f31@mail.gmail.com>

On 1/22/08, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
> I have space on my XP laptop to load VMware Server (which I have done),
> and want to try different versions of Linux on it starting with Kubuntu.
> Has anyone done this?

I've created Linux VMs using the ISO files. But now there are actually
quite a few Linux OS Distro Appliances. If you're not familiar with
the VM Appliances, here's a short article -
http://www.petri.co.il/virtual_top_5_free_vmware_appliances.htm.

One of the nice things about appliances is that you can just use
vmplayer and run whaterver appliance you want. Which makes test
driving Linux distros quick & easy.

Here's the main link for vmware appliances - http://www.vmware.com/appliances/

> Of all the possible downloads, which one(s) should I start with?
>
If you're referring to Linux Distros, that's like asking which flavor
of ice cream should I try at Baskin Robbins. For me, it's the distros
that I consider interesting: CentOS, Gentoo, dyne:bolic. You could
read some of the reviews on distrowatch.com and then find vm
appliances for those distros that tickle your fancy...:-)

I hope that helps.


From russell at personaltelco.net  Tue Jan 22 16:03:54 2008
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 22 Jan 2008 16:03:54 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Installing Kubuntu in VMware on XP
In-Reply-To: <47967BC0.6030907@comcast.net>

>>>>> "Richard" == Richard C Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> writes:

Richard> I have space on my XP laptop to load VMware Server (which I
Richard> have done), and want to try different versions of Linux on it
Richard> starting with Kubuntu.  Has anyone done this?

Richard> The laptop is an Acer Aspire 1640Z with a 1.7 GHz Pentium M
Richard> and 1 GB of RAM. I accepted the defaults when I created the
Richard> virtual machine: 512 MB memory, 8 GB Max hard disk, bridged
Richard> Ethernet.

Richard> What things will I wish I had know after I've started?

I've just started playing with virtualization, and it occurs to me
that since client-bridging on wireless is a bit of a hack when/if it
works, it might be a problem to get network access via your host
system's wireless.  I haven't tried it, so I don't know it won't work,
but I wouldn't be surprised if it doesn't or if it behaves in
unexpected ways.  Nat'ing through your host system (if that's even
possible) should work fine.

In my case, I am using linux/kvm and running windows (not my choice, i
assure you) on top.  W2k's pinball works surprisingly well, even
tunneled over ssh to my desktop.

On Jan 22, 2008 3:26 PM, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
> I have space on my XP laptop to load VMware Server (which I have done),
> and want to try different versions of Linux on it starting with Kubuntu.

If you should end up using a version wit KDE 4.0, keep in mind that
the developers made a deliberate decision to release version 4 before
it was really stable, so your experience may not be representative of
the "normal" kubuntu level of quality.

That said, I have no plans to use [x|k|]ubuntu again -- I've had far,
far to many problems relating to the way the UI seems to be
intertwined with non-ui configurations (power management, wifi, etc..)
 I think debian is a more reliable system, but ubuntu does make more
things work by default if you're happy with Gnome, KDE, or Xfce.

--Rogan

> but I wouldn't be surprised if it doesn't or if it behaves in
> unexpected ways.  Nat'ing through your host system (if that's even
> possible) should work fine.
>
> In my case, I am using linux/kvm and running windows (not my choice, i
> assure you) on top.  W2k's pinball works surprisingly well, even
> tunneled over ssh to my desktop.
>
>
>   
VMWare will only bind to one adapter. It generally selects your primary
one (hardwired port). I "cheated" by adding a second virtual network
tied to my eth1 (wireless port). But I have to switch the VMs to use
that or the primary.

Tim

I run both Kubuntu and Ubuntu, as well as Knoppix and DSL, in VMware
Workstation under Linux and VMware server under Windows XP (work
machine). There are no issues....it just finds everything. I usually
build my hard drives out (allocate all storage now). Some are built as
servers, some as workstations. I haven't had any real problems with 7.04
or 7.10 ('buntu) or anything after Knoppix v3.1 and DSL 3.4+. I don't
normally run sound or plug in anything into USB. Networking and KVM just
work. Make sure you install the VMware tools into the client system though.

From kristian.hermansen at gmail.com  Tue Jan 22 16:23:37 2008
From: kristian.hermansen at gmail.com (Kristian Erik Hermansen)
Date: Tue, 22 Jan 2008 16:23:37 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Installing Kubuntu in VMware on XP
In-Reply-To: <479685E0.2040403@bruce4.com>
References: <47967BC0.6030907@comcast.net> <86prvtl85x.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<479685E0.2040403@bruce4.com>
Message-ID: <fe37588d0801221623o5f0004ebla2bd63eb184130c2@mail.gmail.com>

On Jan 22, 2008 4:10 PM, Tim Bruce <timb at bruce4.com> wrote:
> VMWare will only bind to one adapter. It generally selects your primary
> one (hardwired port). I "cheated" by adding a second virtual network
> tied to my eth1 (wireless port). But I have to switch the VMs to use
> that or the primary.

Not true!  VMware will bind as many as you tell it to :-)  In general,
eth0 is mapped to vmnet0 and eth1 should be mapped to vmnet2.  vmnet1
is automatically mapped to host-only networking.  You can reconfigure
this behavior with vmware-config.pl during installation or at any
time.  I usually map both my wired and wireless interfaces on the same
virtual machine and don't have issues.  One time I mapped 24
interfaces for a project I was doing at Cisco :-)
-- 
Kristian Erik Hermansen
"Know something about everything and everything about something."

On Jan 22, 2008 4:10 PM, Tim Bruce <timb at bruce4.com> wrote:
> VMWare will only bind to one adapter. It generally selects your primary
> one (hardwired port). I "cheated" by adding a second virtual network
> tied to my eth1 (wireless port). But I have to switch the VMs to use

Not entirely true.  There is a utility that is installed but not
placed on the menus
which allows binding to different network cards and even adding more binding
to the config.  This is outside of the vmx files.

With vmplayer installed the path to the utility is:
C:\Program Files\VMware\VMware Player\vmnetcfg.exe

Don't!

1) You don't need to, this particular problem only presents itself to
Windows users, not Linux users.  Many people including myself have
confirmed this.

you're using, why not tell them what you're using or nothing at all?

I would like to have a group of users that can kill processes on a linux 
server without using sudo. I would like them to be able to just run 
'kill <processes ID>' and have it work for any processes.

From griffint at pobox.com  Wed Jan 23 15:01:48 2008
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Wed, 23 Jan 2008 15:01:48 -0800 (PST)

	<87r6g8886u.wl%ckonstanski@pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <2429.204.153.43.29.1201129308.squirrel@webmail.whiz.to>

> At Wed, 23 Jan 2008 14:48:12 -0800,
> Mark Turner wrote:
>>
>> I am stumped with this one...
>> I would like to have a group of users that can kill processes on a linux
>> server without using sudo. I would like them to be able to just run
>> 'kill <processes ID>' and have it work for any processes.
>>
>> This does not sound secure... I know, trust me but its better than
>> putting everyone in the root group.
>>
>> Any idea how I would accomplish this?
>>
>> -Mark
>
> Can you make kill setuid root, or make a kill synonym that is setuid
> root and which calls kill with this privilege?  Just a stab in the
> dark.
>

To limit this to a particular group you'd need a custom version of the
'kill' utility. It would check the group membership before allowing
the operation, and it would have to be setuid root. A small amount
of C programming would be required. Some sloppy C programming could
lead to some interesting exploit opportunities.

Terry



From charlie at schluting.com  Wed Jan 23 15:29:51 2008
From: charlie at schluting.com (Charlie Schluting)
Date: Wed, 23 Jan 2008 15:29:51 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Allow Users to Kill Process

On 1/23/08 2:48 PM, Mark Turner wrote:
> I am stumped with this one...
> I would like to have a group of users that can kill processes on a linux 
> server without using sudo. I would like them to be able to just run 
> 'kill <processes ID>' and have it work for any processes.
> 
> This does not sound secure... I know, trust me but its better than 
> putting everyone in the root group.
> 
> Any idea how I would accomplish this?

But.. this what sudo is for (it's NOT for 'sudo bash'... i.e. providing 
a root shell with your user password). I would allow all people in said 
group access to run the kill command via sudo.

As other people said - just create setuid a C wrapper that takes the PID 
argument and kills it. Only allow people in the desired group to execute it.


  -Charlie

> provided sofware that uses a ncurses gui. From the gui as far as I can

What if you tweak the PATH of the environment this ncurses gui is
running in so that it finds your custom kill script before /bin/kill?
You could then write your own kill that invokes gtksudo or some other
version of sudo that will pop up a dialog for a password.. (or there
are ways to make sudo not require a password)

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Jan 23 18:27:39 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 23 Jan 2008 18:27:39 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] I Need Linux Books (etc)
In-Reply-To: <ebdfd14b0801100755t68ee8bgf8edafcccf505549@mail.gmail.com>
References: <e522eca06b18.47858213@pcc.edu>
	<422ec1510801092336q7ab52d0k80459b5202a05476@mail.gmail.com>
	<ebdfd14b0801100755t68ee8bgf8edafcccf505549@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20080124022739.GA13643@gate.kl-ic.com>


This is a good time to remind PLUG members that we have a library
of mostly new books at http://www.pdxlinux.org/wiki/PlugLibrary . 

You probably won't find the particular textbook your instructor
assigned.  It is usually a good idea to buy the textbook, perhaps
used, there are websites for that.  If you are generous, you can 
donate it to the library when you are done.  The assigned text may
not have all the information you need, but it will have most of it.

However, the PLUG library contains many books covering some of the
same subjects, at least a chapter or two, and sometimes a different
author or treatment will help you understand a particular topic that
the textbook author cannot explain well.  Most of the time, there is
some book or journal article that explains the topic better than the
textbook author(s) do.  After all, 90% of what a textbook writer
writes about is not their speciality.  It is often better to read
what the authors read in order to write the textbook.  It is better
still to read newer work;  the textbook writer drew from materials
older than the textbook.  The new stuff is in the PLUG library.

Please read the instructions on the wiki, and let me know a day or
two in advance, and I will bring them to a meeting.  At the very
low rates books are being checked out, you probably can keep the
book a long time before someone else wants it.  I would like to
get these books out of my basement and in use!

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs


From tsharples at qorvus.com  Wed Jan 23 20:20:53 2008
From: tsharples at qorvus.com (Tom Sharples)
Date: Wed, 23 Jan 2008 20:20:53 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Allow Users to Kill Process
In-Reply-To: <4797C42C.3040507@bendbulletin.com>
References: <4797C42C.3040507@bendbulletin.com>
Message-ID: <F2445115BFF449E7A9399D92F39E8E29@tsharplesoffic>

Why not write two scripts: one that takes your user's input and copies it to 
a file /tmp/process_to_be_killed, and a second (with root privileges)  that 
runs as a cronjob every minute or so, looks at the contents, if any, of 
/tmp/process_to_be_killed, and kills those processes? You can get a little 
more sophisticated and have said cronjob look first at a list of jobs that 
are not permitted be killed (maintained by you), prior to taking action.

Tom S.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Thu Jan 24 10:33:52 2008
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Thu, 24 Jan 2008 10:33:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Allow Users to Kill Process
In-Reply-To: <4797D4F8.1040406@bendbulletin.com>
References: <4797C42C.3040507@bendbulletin.com>
	<4797CDEF.5020003@schluting.com> <4797D4F8.1040406@bendbulletin.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0801241033o5a7b83e1xc9955a720621c452@mail.gmail.com>

On Jan 23, 2008 3:59 PM, Mark Turner <mturner at bendbulletin.com> wrote:
>
> I would love to use sudo, it is just not an option. We have some vendor
> provided sofware that uses a ncurses gui. From the gui as far as I can
> tell it tries to kill processes when users try to cancel "jobs". I
> cannot change the command used to kill the processes.... hence this
> email. It would be wonderful if I could just change it to use sudo
> /bin/kill rather then /bin/kill.
>

First, does it call the kill binary or use the kill system call?  If
it does the kill system call, you will have a harder time wrapping it.
 Probably have to use LD_PRELOAD libc wrapper.  Which kill binary does
it call?  One possibility is to replace the binary it calls with a
shell script that does "sudo /bin/kill.real".  If it just does "kill",
then setting the PATH like somebody suggested so it uses your wrapper
script will work.

BTW, you can configure sudo so that users in a group can only run
"kill".  You can even configure it so that they don't need to enter
their password.

 - Ian

From plug-ng1 at MartinConsulting.com  Fri Jan 25 10:15:15 2008
From: plug-ng1 at MartinConsulting.com (Brian Martin)
Date: Fri, 25 Jan 2008 10:15:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] xorg mouse pointer shows in wrong place
Message-ID: <000001c85f7e$3bbc18c0$b3344a40$@com>

I have a small system running OpenSUSE 10.3 with Xorg-X11-server 7.2-143.9.
My graphics card is an ATI Radeon X1050 -- not the greatest card but good
enough for my purposes.  My monitor is a Westinghouse wide screen 47" LCD.
After installing the card, the screen was coming up in 1280x1024 resolution.
Things were looking a little stretched horizontally, but otherwise it was
working well.  The mouse worked fine.

I edited /etc/X11/xorg.conf to change the resolution to 1920x1080.  Now the
image looks great - it fills the screen top to bottom and side to side with
no horizontal stretching.  The only problem is with the mouse.  The mouse
pointer on the screen doesn't correspond to the where Xorg thinks it is.
Horizontally it's fine, but vertically I have to position the mouse pointer
farther down from the top than the item I want to click on by about a factor
of 2.  So, if there's an icon 3 inches down from the top of the screen that
I want to click on, I have to place the mouse pointer 6 inches down the
screen to hit it.  For things on the bottom half of the screen, the mouse
pointer actually has to move completely below the bottom edge of the screen
(which it will do, logically at least).  Horizontal positioning is fine.

I've been searching and tweaking without much success.  Can someone give me
some help on how to get the visible mouse pointer and the effective mouse
pointer sync'd?

The xorg.conf and log files are here:  http://www.martinconsulting.com/xorg

                   -Brian Martin

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Fri Jan 25 11:13:45 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Fri, 25 Jan 2008 11:13:45 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] xorg mouse pointer shows in wrong place
In-Reply-To: <000001c85f7e$3bbc18c0$b3344a40$@com>
References: <000001c85f7e$3bbc18c0$b3344a40$@com>
Message-ID: <200801251113.45239.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Brian Martin
# on Friday 25 January 2008 10:15:

> graphics card is an ATI Radeon X1050

Got a link to online docs for that driver?  All I get from google is 
wikis.

>I edited /etc/X11/xorg.conf to change the resolution to 1920x1080. 
...
> The mouse pointer on the screen doesn't correspond to the
> where Xorg thinks it is.

  (**) Input device list set to "/dev/gpmdata,/dev/input/mice"
  ...
  (==) fglrx(0): Silken mouse enabled
  (==) fglrx(0): Using hardware cursor
  ...
  (**) Mouse[1]: Device: "/dev/input/mice"

I would investigate whether gpm is causing trouble (though it looks like 
gpmdata is unused.)

And/or whether there's anything in the ATI driver for hardware/software 
cursor settings.

The fact that the cursor only *displays* in the wrong place implies that 
it is likely a problem in the display driver.

There are a lot of options set in your video driver section.  Without 
some docs, it is tough to say whether one of those is causing trouble.

I've also never seen the InputFashion option.  New in 7.2?

--Eric

From lnxknight at gmail.com  Sun Jan 27 21:31:07 2008
From: lnxknight at gmail.com (Matt McKenzie)
Date: Sun, 27 Jan 2008 21:31:07 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Disk wiping utility?

	<479D5FCA.7040102@cad2cam.com>
Message-ID: <da2207740801272131r71996472se6640773a687ea40@mail.gmail.com>

>
> kurt braget wrote:
> > I'm running out of memory on my laptop, which is partitioned with
> openSUSE
> > 10.3 and Windows. What I'd like to do is wipe the drive and do a clean
> > install of openSUSE 10.3 so I can use the whole hard drive. I don't even
> use
> > Windows on this machine, and I want to experiment with formatting the
> entire
> > hard drive. Anybody know of a good utility I could use to do this? Any
> > warnings? Thanks.
> > _______________________________________________
>

Well you could just use the disk partitioning tools during the SuSE
installer, and just repartition and wipe the drive.
If you don't care about the old data possibly being retrieved later (i.e.
it's just you using the machine), this should be sufficient.

If you want to be more thorough, you could use a DoD type tool.
My favorite, useful when customers want their drive wiped, is Boot & Nuke,
which is on the UltimateBootCD (ultimatebootcd.com).
It is a mini Linux distro itself, and is text based menu driven, it is
capable of DoD level hard drive wipes, with random or NULL data, multiple
passes, etc.  Basically just select the level of wipe, and fire 'n forget.

----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight

From plug-ng1 at MartinConsulting.com  Mon Jan 28 01:17:20 2008
From: plug-ng1 at MartinConsulting.com (Brian Martin)
Date: Mon, 28 Jan 2008 01:17:20 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] xorg mouse pointer shows in wrong place - resolved
In-Reply-To: <000a01c85f92$1d8c5860$58a50920$@com>
References: <000001c85f7e$3bbc18c0$b3344a40$@com>	<fe37588d0801251109n51628d83s95f57f2ddf0f81b1@mail.gmail.com>
	<000a01c85f92$1d8c5860$58a50920$@com>
Message-ID: <000a01c8618e$95dae4a0$c190ade0$@com>

 resolved my mouse pointer problem, but without really knowing what caused
it.

The original sequence of events was:

1) The SUSE installation created an xorg.conf file, with a resolution on my
monitor of 1280x1024.
2) I followed the procedures to install the ATI driver, in which their tool
updated xorg.conf, but still left it with a resolution of 1280x1024.
3) I changed the resolution to 1920x1080, and my mouse pointer graphic
didn't match where the OS thought it was.

I resolved the problem by going back to the original xorg.conf, and swapping
steps 2 and 3.  So I changed the resolution before running their tool to
configure xorg.conf for their driver.

The result was a radically different xorg.conf that works fine.  The working
copy is available at www.martinconsulting.com/xorg/xorg.conf.works for
anyone that's interested.

Thanks for your help, everyone.

                -Brian Martin

From bsr at spek.org  Mon Jan 28 06:59:12 2008
From: bsr at spek.org (Brent Rieck)
Date: Mon, 28 Jan 2008 06:59:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] How MUAs See Attachment Size
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0801280618150.11130@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0801280618150.11130@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <479DEDC0.9020204@spek.org>

Rich Shepard wrote:
>    This is probably something I should know, but I don't. I'm curious why a
> 40.9M tarball is seen by postfix (and pine) as 56M large when attached to an
> email message. The size discrepancy is more than the difference between 1000
> and 1024.

I believe it's because of mime encoding, translating the 8 
bit-per-character tar file into a 7-bit-per-character attachment 
increases the total number of bytes needed to represent the file.

--Brent


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Jan 28 09:19:29 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 28 Jan 2008 09:19:29 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Sharing an offsite virtual machine as a VPN hub
Message-ID: <20080128171928.GA30867@gate.kl-ic.com>


I have a rented offsite virtual machine (Xen) for various tasks.  I
use it to set up VPN tunnels to my various sites, and to my laptop
when I am out.  This allows me to build stable connections to my home
network on a dynamic IP address and behind a firewall, for example.
The virtual has a second IP address, with port 80 on that address
connected to a VPN port.  That means I can run a VPN from my laptop
through an offsite firewall that is configured to block all but web
traffic (Providence Medical Center in NE Portland is like that).

OpenVPN can be configured to do this for many, many networks.  A
mimimum virtual machine costs $20/month from many places, and extra
IP addresses are typically $1/month .  With routing set up properly,
the virtual networks can be kept separate for each person.  The only
practical limit to how many ways you can split one of these "VPN 
hubs" is security - eventually, the crowd will get large enough to
include someone untrustworthy.

I can imagine 5 people splitting one of these virtual machines, and
paying $5/month for the ability to VPN into their machines through
a remote machine, with a dedicated port 80 inbound address for each
person.  Without the port 80 kluge, it would be cheaper. 

I would like people to have something like this for the Linux Clinic,
or for Linux Workshops, so that we can reach out and touch people's
machines for software installs, debugging, and the like.  In those
cases, the connection from home/office to the hub can be activated
only temporarily, for the duration of the clinic.  The connection 
is initiated from the home/office, not from the hub, so the time
exposure of the home/office networks can be limited.

I'm not going to run something like this, but I wouldn't mind helping
set it up for someone else to run, if we can use it for the clinic. 
I can imagine it becoming a paying business for someone doing remote
maintenance of Linux boxen under contract, so there are other reasons
to consider getting involved.  Anybody want to spearhead this?

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From kristian.hermansen at gmail.com  Mon Jan 28 11:39:35 2008
From: kristian.hermansen at gmail.com (Kristian Erik Hermansen)
Date: Mon, 28 Jan 2008 11:39:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Disk wiping utility?
In-Reply-To: <479E253E.4050206@comcast.net>

On Jan 28, 2008 10:55 AM, Michael Ewan <mhewan1 at comcast.net> wrote:
> This level of disk wipe does not really delete the data, you need a
> minimum of 5 passes, 3 passes random data, one pass all 1's, one pass
> all zeros.

You are incorrect sir.  The 'Guttmann' paper only applies to older
drives.  The myth about recovering data after /dev/zero is false and
is a very old DoD standard that applies to only certain types of drive
encoding.  Read the paper.  Read the rebuttals.  The rest is hype...

Kristian Erik Hermansen

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Mon Jan 28 11:42:49 2008
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Mon, 28 Jan 2008 11:42:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Disk wiping utility?
In-Reply-To: <479E253E.4050206@comcast.net>
References: <422ec1510801272039k5c2e449fo31064818d61aefd4@mail.gmail.com>
	<fe37588d0801272242w358bc04bp607aaeed3edce464@mail.gmail.com>
	<479E253E.4050206@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <ca5165530801281142r41f96d6ct55a52850565f22a9@mail.gmail.com>

On Jan 28, 2008 10:55 AM, Michael Ewan <mhewan1 at comcast.net> wrote:
> Kristian Erik Hermansen wrote:
> > On Jan 27, 2008 8:39 PM, kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> wrote:
> >
> >> I'm running out of memory on my laptop, which is partitioned with openSUSE
> >> 10.3 and Windows. What I'd like to do is wipe the drive and do a clean
> >> install of openSUSE 10.3 so I can use the whole hard drive. I don't even use
> >> Windows on this machine, and I want to experiment with formatting the entire
> >> hard drive. Anybody know of a good utility I could use to do this? Any
> >> warnings? Thanks.
> >>
> >
> > # while true; do dd if=/dev/zero of=/dev/sdx; done
> >
> This level of disk wipe does not really delete the data, you need a
> minimum of 5 passes, 3 passes random data, one pass all 1's, one pass
> all zeros.
>

While true that you need multiple passes, the data that is sent in gets run
through a scrambler then a NRZ function to reduce the frequency range.
At one time you could use a scope and read the data patterns from the device.
Not any more.  If you really wanted to make sure; you would reverse engineer the
polynomial that is being used for both the CRC and the scrambler to find the
patterns that give the highest and lowest frequencies written to the disk.

Also be aware that the new SATA drives also support the same function
as SCSI/Fibre
channel drives do, what used to be called CE space.

On Mon, 28 Jan 2008, kurt braget wrote:

> I would have tried to repartition the drive but I'm not exactly sure how
> to do that.

Kurt,

   When you initiate the installation of a linux distribution, all (most?)
first ask you how you want the available drive space partitioned. You can
also use fdisk/cfdisk to see what's there, and change it if desired.

   Of course, any time you repartition (unless you use a tool like
PartitionMagic), data are wiped out.

On Jan 28, 2008 3:03 PM, Kristian Erik Hermansen <
kristian.hermansen at gmail.com> wrote:

> On Jan 28, 2008 2:53 PM, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> >    Of course, any time you repartition (unless you use a tool like
> > PartitionMagic), data are wiped out.
>
> Data is not wiped even if you use gparted on most Linux installers.
> You can even resize NTFS without losing data.  The partition may be
> altered/resized/deleted, but the data is still there of course :-)
> --
>

Exactly.  The repartitioning step just rewrites the partition table.
It is the next step, in most installers, that formats the selected
partitions.
Granted if you alter the partition table, it can make it more difficult to
get to the data, but it is still there if you know how to get to it.

Depending on the tool used, you can usually tell the installer to format
/dev/sd[x] and not format /dev/sd[y], for example in my case I have a
separate /home partition that I don't format when upgrading/reinstalling.  I
just did this recently when installing Fedora 8 fresh over F7.  Going from
F6 -> F7 I did an upgrade, and it got a little messy, mainly because of
using livna 3rd party repository.

In the OP's case you would just set all the partitions to format, and it
would've "wiped" the old data, though not to DoD standards.
It sounds like the OP wanted to accomplish 2 things here- wipe out Windowz,
and learn about data wiping tools.  So mission accomplished, it sounds
like?  Tell us how the SuSE install goes.

-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight

Base64 encoding typically increases the size of attached binary files  
by ~20-30%.

Comcast had an outage in my area this afternoon. When service was restored, I could not connect to the Internet through my router; I had to run an Ethernet cable from my laptop to the cable modem. Comcast was of no help; they don't support routers.

I telnet-ed in to the router's manager menu (Netgear RT-314) and ran the status routine from the system maintenance menu. It showed the WAN port status as 10M/Half, and showed a few packets being sent and received between the cable modem and the router (this while the Ethernet cable was still connected to the router). However, it showed no IP address for the WAN port. BTW, when the cable is no longer connecting the router to the cable modem, the status of the WAN port reads Down.

Now, with the cable connected to the laptop, I find that the laptop does have an IP address, and that I can send this e-mail.

Any recommendations on where to dig next to figure out what happened, and how to correct it will be greatly appreciated.

 Disk wiping usually means making the prior content unrecoverable. If
you mean you want to repartition and reformat a HD then you can use
partition magic or even an install CD/DVD for linux or GNU Parted.

http://www.tech-faq.com/free-partition-software.shtml

By memory do you mean HD space? If you need more memory for the computer
then check to see if your computer can accept more physical memory.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Jan 30 00:00:42 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)

Subject: [PLUG] Command line and paths

This has me shaking my head. Hopefully someone can explain what is going on.

I have a Java application that I run on Ubuntu Gutsy x86_64 using Sun Java 1.5. Actually I have several Javas installed, but that is the one that is set as the default, and the application won't run without it being the default. Luckily no other application cares, so all is well.

The problem is that the application has a number of buttons which are displayed with the use of icon files. The icon files are all in the correct folder, but the icons have never displayed. All I get is a little white box where the button is supposed to be. There are also popups explaining what the boxes are, so it is not critical that I have the buttons, but it would be nice to have them.

This evening I got an e-mail from the developer saying that he had figured out that to get the icons to appear you have to launch it from the command line while being in the folder where the .jar file is located. Thus, I need to:

cd ~/Software/TreeForm102
java -jar TreeForm.jar

This works great. The application launches and the buttons appear. 

But it's a lot easier to launch it from the Gnome panel application launcher. I had a launcher for it previously (which did not display the buttons), so I edited it so that the command line is "/home/jjj/Software/TreeForm102/java -jar TreeForm.jar." But after doing so it still launches without the buttons.

So then I went back to the command line and changed to my home folder. Then I entered the same command, but including the path in the command. It launches without the buttons. To clarify:

This command launches it with the buttons:
jjj at Devil7:~/Software/TreeForm102$ java -jar TreeForm.jar

If I change to ~/ and launch it with this command it launches without the buttons:
jjj at Devil7:~java -jar /home/jjj/Software/TreeForm102/TreeForm.jar

Why should it make a difference if I am in the folder or if I specify the folder by stating the full path?

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Jan 30 00:24:01 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 30 Jan 2008 00:24:01 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Command line and paths
In-Reply-To: <20080130000042.c08839d1.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080130000042.c08839d1.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <c834ec80801300024w164f6f26l9a0d369f2729359a@mail.gmail.com>

On Jan 30, 2008 12:00 AM, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> This evening I got an e-mail from the developer saying that he had figured out
> that to get the icons to appear you have to launch it from the command line
> while being in the folder where the .jar file is located. Thus, I need to:
>

This app is using relative paths to find the image files -- that is,
instead of looking for
"/home/jjj/Software/TreeForm102/buttons/button.jpg" it's looking for
"buttons/button.jpg" (for example).

The directory that you start the application from is the location that
these relative paths are resolved from, so if you launch treeform from
your home directory, it will look for images in
/home/jjj/buttons/button.jpg (following along with my fictional
example)

> cd ~/Software/TreeForm102
> java -jar TreeForm.jar

By changing directories you've changed the location that is used to
resolve relative paths, so now treeform is looking in the correct
place.

> But it's a lot easier to launch it from the Gnome panel application launcher. I
> had a launcher for it previously (which did not display the buttons), so I edited it
> so that the command line is "/home/jjj/Software/TreeForm102/java -jar
> TreeForm.jar." But after doing so it still launches without the buttons.

Generally, you can set the directory that an application runs from
when you create an application launcher, but when I just fired up
gnome-panel and tried it I didn't see anything related to this.  (I'll
try not to rant about gnome.)

As Kristian pointed out, you can also create a simple shell script
(say, Treeform.sh):

#!/bin/bash
cd ~/Software/TreeForm102
java -jar TreeForm.jar

make that executable:

chmod a+x Treeform.sh

and point your launcher at that.

> If I change to ~/ and launch it with this command it launches without the
> buttons:
> jjj at Devil7:~java -jar /home/jjj/Software/TreeForm102/TreeForm.jar

This doesn't change the directory you're *in* when you launch it -- it
just tells Java where to find the jar in excruciating detail.

--Rogan

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Jan 30 06:34:00 2008
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 30 Jan 2008 06:34:00 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Command line and paths
In-Reply-To: <20080130000042.c08839d1.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080130000042.c08839d1.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0801300628180.5211@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 30 Jan 2008, John Jason Jordan wrote:

> So then I went back to the command line and changed to my home folder.
> Then I entered the same command, but including the path in the command. It
> launches without the buttons. To clarify:
>
> This command launches it with the buttons:
> jjj at Devil7:~/Software/TreeForm102$ java -jar TreeForm.jar
>
> If I change to ~/ and launch it with this command it launches without the buttons:
> jjj at Devil7:~java -jar /home/jjj/Software/TreeForm102/TreeForm.jar

> Why should it make a difference if I am in the folder or if I specify the
> folder by stating the full path?

   To keep the shell doing what you want, consider creating a subdirectory in
your home directory called, for example, shell-scripts. In this directory
write a three-line script for your java apps and others you want to invoke
from an icon or menu. Make the script executable (e.g., 'chmod 755
treeform.sh'), then call the script from your icon.

   The treeform.sh script would look like this:

cd Software/TreeForm102
java -jar TreeForm.jar
cd

   When run, the PWD will change to the Software/TreeForm102 directory, then
invoke the java application from within that directory. When you exit, the
PWD returns to your home directory.

Rich

> through an offsite firewall that is configured to block all but web

Do you use port 80 TCP or UDP?
Does Providence Medical Center allow port 80 UDP?


> OpenVPN can be configured to do this for many, many networks.  A
> mimimum virtual machine costs $20/month from many places, and extra
> IP addresses are typically $1/month .  With routing set up properly,
> the virtual networks can be kept separate for each person.  The only
> practical limit to how many ways you can split one of these "VPN 
> hubs" is security - eventually, the crowd will get large enough to
> include someone untrustworthy.
> 
> I can imagine 5 people splitting one of these virtual machines, and
> paying $5/month for the ability to VPN into their machines through
> a remote machine, with a dedicated port 80 inbound address for each
> person.  Without the port 80 kluge, it would be cheaper. 
> 
> I would like people to have something like this for the Linux Clinic,
> or for Linux Workshops, so that we can reach out and touch people's
> machines for software installs, debugging, and the like.  In those
> cases, the connection from home/office to the hub can be activated
> only temporarily, for the duration of the clinic.  The connection 
> is initiated from the home/office, not from the hub, so the time
> exposure of the home/office networks can be limited.
> 
> I'm not going to run something like this, but I wouldn't mind helping
> set it up for someone else to run, if we can use it for the clinic. 
> I can imagine it becoming a paying business for someone doing remote
> maintenance of Linux boxen under contract, so there are other reasons
> to consider getting involved.  Anybody want to spearhead this?

My company developed this about three years ago. At the time, our
application was protecting children on the Internet by separating
them from the raw Internet. It failed to sell because nobody was
interested but the software is still there.

>>>>> "Richard" == Richard C Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> writes:

Richard> And, which routers do you folks prefer? I'll consider adding
Richard> wireless capability, too.

For me, the ability to run my own linux-based firmware on the thing is
paramount, so I would pick something with that in mind.  Of those, I'd
go for one of these:

  Linksys WRT54GL 
  ASUS WL-500g Premium

The Linksys is the currently available version that still is
practically reflashable (as opposed to most of the off-the-shelf
versions you are going to find, er, on a shelf today).  The ASUS is
not a device I've ever had my hands on, but they are well-regarded and
also have features common to one I have used extensively: a mini-PCI
slot which allows you to change the radio; and 2 USB2 host ports,
which lets you plug stuff in.  Bear in mind these are consumer-grade
devices that might fail suddenly for no obvious reason, but usually
don't.

For others, see:

  http://wiki.openwrt.org/TableOfHardware

Another more expensive device that I like are the Soekris boards.  PTP
uses them for outdoor wireless gear (in a suitable NEMA enclosure).

  http://www.soekris.com/

There are other similar products as well.

You need to find out where Mozilla, Firefox, Netscape, or Opera is
installed to.  It isn't /usr/lib/mozilla.  If you use firefox, run
this:

ls -l `which firefox`   #(note that those are *backticks* not single quotes)

and see where the symlink points--you'll only need the directory. (on
my ubuntu box, it's /usr/lib/firefox)

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Wed Jan 30 13:57:24 2008
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Wed, 30 Jan 2008 13:57:24 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] 'cd ~' at the end of bash scripts
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0801301120220.5211@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <20080130000042.c08839d1.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<c834ec80801301110k721f191dk9f13bb10d050b5e@mail.gmail.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0801301120220.5211@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <200801301357.24861.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Rich Shepard
# on Wednesday 30 January 2008 11:21:

>> I'm a bit fuzzy on how scripts work if they don't have a sh-bang
>> (#!/path/to/interpreter), but isn't the trailing "cd" is
>> unnecessary.
>
>Rogan,
>
>? ?Good point. Most of my shell scripts have the sh-bang on the first
> line, but some don't (soffice.sh, for example). All explicitly return
> to ~/ without harm or foul.

I can't see why they should.  If it is 'source'd, then any `cd`s within 
the script would impact the calling shell (which may not be in ~ -- and 
thus it should be remembering the initial $PWD and trying to return to 
that -- though that may not exist by that point (lots of fun exercises 
for the reader there), but to keep it simple: 'source'd scripts should 
really just not cd at all.)

If it is not sourced, then the subprocess is just trying to `cd` 
immediately before termination.  This hides the exit code of (what 
would otherwise be) the last command from the caller and instead 
returns the exit code of `cd ~`.

So, if the script just does environment setup and then runs a command, 
this practice masks that command's exit code and is likely to bite 
someone doing e.g. "for i in *; do some_wrapper.sh $i || break; done".

AFAIK, whether it has a shebang has nothing to do with any of that.

--Eric
-- 
I arise in the morning torn between a desire to improve the world and a
desire to enjoy the world. This makes it hard to plan the day.
--E.B. White
---------------------------------------------------
    http://scratchcomputing.com
---------------------------------------------------

# from Robert Citek
# on Wednesday 30 January 2008 14:48:

>On Jan 30, 2008 4:32 PM, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>> On Wed, 30 Jan 2008, Eric Wilhelm wrote:
>> > I can't see why they should.
>>
>> Because I explicitly put 'cd ' as the last line in each script.
>> Should have mentioned that more explicitly.
>
>If you run the script, it doesn't matter.  It only matters when you
>source the script, and you really shouldn't be sourcing scripts.

As I said:  it does matter if you expect to get a meaningful return code 
from the script.

If it "doesn't matter" (aka: "does no good") to end the script with `cd 
~` and it gets in the way of returning the exit status, then why do it?

If I always use the side door, does it matter that my front walk has 
broken glass on it?

> Richard> And, which routers do you folks prefer? I'll consider adding

> For me, the ability to run my own linux-based firmware on the thing is
> paramount, so I would pick something with that in mind.  Of those, I'd
> go for one of these:
>
>   Linksys WRT54GL 
>   ASUS WL-500g Premium
>
> The Linksys is the currently available version that still is
> practically reflashable (as opposed to most of the off-the-shelf
> versions you are going to find, er, on a shelf today).  The ASUS is
> not a device I've ever had my hands on, but they are well-regarded and
> also have features common to one I have used extensively: a mini-PCI
> slot which allows you to change the radio; and 2 USB2 host ports,
> which lets you plug stuff in.  Bear in mind these are consumer-grade
> devices that might fail suddenly for no obvious reason, but usually
> don't.
>
> For others, see:
>
>   http://wiki.openwrt.org/TableOfHardware
>
> Another more expensive device that I like are the Soekris boards.  PTP
> uses them for outdoor wireless gear (in a suitable NEMA enclosure).
>
>   http://www.soekris.com/
>
> There are other similar products as well.
>
>
>   
I strongly recommend the Linksys WRT54G (apparently L is the FLOSS 
version). Secure, cheap, fast, and easy to manage. Linksys provides 
timely firmware updates, and as Russell says, they actively support Linux.

>>>>> "Michael" == Michael Ewan <mhewan1 at comcast.net> writes:

Michael> I strongly recommend the Linksys WRT54G (apparently L is the
Michael> FLOSS version). Secure, cheap, fast, and easy to
Michael> manage. Linksys provides timely firmware updates, and as
Michael> Russell says, they actively support Linux.

Older WRT54G's (like several years ago) were reflashable, the current
ones are not anymore[1].  Start with v5 they used something other than
linux and also cut the amount of ram and flash.  However, to satisfy
the reflashers they also started selling the WRT54GL which is
approximately the same as a WRT54G v4, which is reflashable.

[1] "not" in a "practical" sense.  you can still reflash them, but the
    space available is so limited, the utility of the resulting device
    is very low.  In other words, "it doesn't work".

russell at personaltelco.net

else seems to go down. I've tested this several times with 4 separate
laptops, on several different occasions. This is probably a separate topic,
but here is a scenario: I check the AP's on 2 - 4 different laptops,

become visible (confirmed on multiple laptops). Right now, for example,
Metro-fi and Metro-fi premium are up (which are apparently the same AP). If
I connect to Metro-fi, eventually it will go down, then 2 - 4 new access
points will go up, in addition to Metro-fi, yet, Metro-fi will not deliver
internet. Moral of the story is, maybe there is some frequency issue going
on in my area, but I'm not sure how to test for that (really want to learn
though, so point me to a reference or tool-set if you know of one). If there
is a frequency issue, I may not need a new router. I would like to try a
Linksys though, as I've never had any trouble with them.

Yeah, even with a comcast account, I'm still complaining about Metro-fi ;)

Kurt

wrong but I'm going to posit that its probably channels banging against each
other, I don't know enough about Metro Fi or the wireless situation around
your area so someone may have more info then me. I know when I started
getting neighbors moving into my apartment complex who added wireless I had
to change channels a few times because my AP kept dropping because we were
using the same frequency.

Regarding purchasing a wireless router, if want a WRT54GL or the Asus 
router, you will probably have to mail order it.  For other computer 
stuff, try Pacific Solutions(pacificsolutions.com) at 5118 SE Powell 
Blvd.  They are very Linux friendly.  There is also Belmont 
Computers(belmontcomputers) at 4505 SE Belmont St.  After that, it's 
Freddy's or the big box stores farther east.  BTW, none of the 
Freddy's carry much in the way of computer gear, it's mostly 
accessories(input devices, cables, blank media, etc.).  However, you 
should know that the Hawthorne store is particularly small.  Other 
Freddy's are typically larger.

December 27th, 2007  2008: Linux?s year on the desktop Posted by Robin Harris @ 4:04 pm Categories: Infrastructure,  Software Tags: Desktop, AsusTek Computer, Microsoft Corp., Linux, UNIX, Operating Systems, Open Source, Software, Robin Harris                   298 TalkBacks

                                                              Desktop Linux hurts Microsoft
 Linux has kept a big chunk of the server business out of Microsoft?s hands. But in 2008, Linux will hurt Microsoft on the desktop. Here?s how.
 A new computing platform
 Thanks to Moore?s Law and evolving application needs, a new computing platform arrives every 10 years. Mainframes in the ?50s, minicomputers in the ?60s, PCs in the ?70s, microcontrollers in the ?80s, PDAs and cell phones in the ?90s and now sub-$400 - soon to be sub-$300 notebooks. 
 Small and light enough to be carried everywhere, these sub-notes provide Internet access, PDA functionality and basic mail and document creation functionality at a rock-bottom price. Asustek is expected to build 1,000,000 Eee sub-notes in Q1 ?08 alone. Asustek?s competitors are just getting warmed up.
 What can Microsoft do?
 Microsoft has gotten fat on the $50 Windows tax it charges PC manufacturers. But on a razor-thin margin vendors can?t afford Windows.
 So they?re going with Linux. If Asustek sells 5 million Eee?s, and their competitors sell another 5 million, several million consumers will be introduced to desktop Linux for the first time.
 And millions of copies of Windows and Office won?t be sold.
 Microsoft will skate in ?08
 For all the attention Apple gets for its growing market share, the Linux-based sub-$400 notebook/sub-$200 desktop unit sales will be several times as large in 2008. Even combined this won?t hurt Microsoft in 2008, thanks to the growing migration to Vista.
 2009 is a different story. 25 million Linux desktops will take a bite out of Microsoft sales - one that Wall Street will certainly notice. 
 The Storage Bits take
 Microsoft?s days as a de facto monopoly are coming to an end - and not a moment too soon. Increasingly desperate attacks on Linux will intensify, but how does Microsoft go after Wal-Mart?
 Comments welcome, of course. How should Microsoft respond to very-low-cost Linux systems?

From Jason.VanCleve at chrome.com  Wed Jan 30 15:14:24 2008
From: Jason.VanCleve at chrome.com (Jason Van Cleve)
Date: Wed, 30 Jan 2008 15:14:24 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Command line and paths
In-Reply-To: <20080130090909.2475af82.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080130000042.c08839d1.johnxj@comcast.net><c834ec80801300024w164f6f26l9a0d369f2729359a@mail.gmail.com>
	<20080130090909.2475af82.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <1DEC803BC73785498C98EF74AA6ECFEA01AC66B2@pdxmsx01.chrome.dt.inc>

John,

Java applications have a couple of file-system references at their
disposal:  the current working directory (cwd) and the "classpath".
Files can be found relative to those two things or relative to the root
(which latter is to be avoided, for portability reasons).  What it can't
do so easily is find out the path to the command that invokes it.  In
this case, as others have mentioned, it's probably finding the images
relative to the cwd.  So if you aren't invoking it from where the JAR is
(the "cd" solution), the develop can't "fix" it without some work.

However, you might pass this along to him:  if he packs those image
files into a JAR (either the main executable JAR or another in the same
directory), then he'll be able to load them via the classpath, and you
won't have this problem any more.  Obviously it's better if all his
users don't have to write shell scripts to run his program, as you've
had to do.

I can explain how to accomplish that, if the developer is interested.

--Jason

From russell at personaltelco.net  Thu Jan 31 09:39:41 2008
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 31 Jan 2008 09:39:41 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Interference, was Preferred Routers, was Router Problem
In-Reply-To: <422ec1510801310157v7278f7cfs19324546a18815e8@mail.gmail.com>
References: <013020080423.21831.479FFBD2000B0BAD00005547220073747800000A9B9C9D@comcast.net>
	<e2aeccd60801292128x4b73cfd9pef1a07e8eafd306d@mail.gmail.com>
	<e2aeccd60801292129q1a481461ud7c8a88e97741b72@mail.gmail.com>
	<47A018DE.7070008@comcast.net>
	<e2aeccd60801300142h44d39483x35dfad79ceaaf5c1@mail.gmail.com>
	<47A0A068.3040107@comcast.net>
	<422ec1510801310157v7278f7cfs19324546a18815e8@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <86d4rhyjvm.fsf_-_@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "kurt" == kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> writes:

kurt> One weird thing about wireless in my area: if metro-fi goes
kurt> down, everything else seems to go down. I've tested this several
kurt> times with 4 separate laptops, on several different
kurt> occasions. This is probably a separate topic, but here is a
kurt> scenario: I check the AP's on 2 - 4 different laptops, Metro-fi
kurt> and Metro-fi premium are up. Metro-fi goes down, and new AP's
kurt> become visible (confirmed on multiple laptops). Right now, for
kurt> example, Metro-fi and Metro-fi premium are up (which are
kurt> apparently the same AP). If I connect to Metro-fi, eventually it
kurt> will go down, then 2 - 4 new access points will go up, in
kurt> addition to Metro-fi, yet, Metro-fi will not deliver
kurt> internet. Moral of the story is, maybe there is some frequency
kurt> issue going on in my area, but I'm not sure how to test for that
kurt> (really want to learn though, so point me to a reference or
kurt> tool-set if you know of one). If there is a frequency issue, I
kurt> may not need a new router. I would like to try a Linksys though,
kurt> as I've never had any trouble with them.

That sounds a lot like an interference problem.  There are only three
non-overlapping channels: 1, 6, and 11.  That means the some (though
not much) of the power transmitted on channel 1 will be audible on
channel 5.  Naturally, the closer you are to the transmit channel, the
more this cross-channel interference you'll hear.  That fact pushes
most, but not all people on to 1, 6 and 11.  Some people operate on 3
or 9 or some other channel because it lets them hear their own AP
better amongst the noise, while generating noise for two of their
neighboring "non-overlapping" channels.

As for checking for interference, my first knee-jerk suggestion is
kismet.  It will show you which APs are present on which channels, and
also which ones are audibly active from your location.  

Kismet[1] (properly configured) will show you 802.11 traffic.  There
are other users of 2.4GHz spectrum though.  Like, lots.  There are
cordless phones, microwaves, bluetooth, etc, etc.  One tool that
purports to provide information on all of that is something called a
wi-spy[2].  It is a small USB dongle and software that will generate a
plot of rf energy by frequency across the unlicensed 2.4 GHz band.
Personal Telco has talked about buying one of these at various times,
but hasn't.  Partly because one of our members has one already and
we've been able to borrow it in a pinch.  But, having to borrow it,
means that it isn't readily at hand, and we've got to have sufficient
"activation energy"[3] to follow through on it.  They used to be
reasonably cheap (around $100), but recently about doubled in price.
You can get one with a detachable antenna for about $400 now.

[1] http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kismet_%28software%29
[2] http://www.metageek.net/products/wi-spy24x
[3] http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Activation_energy


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

your first onboard serial port (what DOS and windows would call Com1).


From donkjunk at softhome.net  Thu Jan 31 11:06:13 2008

People have been saying 'this is the year of linux' almost every year since 
red hat first went public. I don't believe there will ever be an official 
'year of linux' but that either one of two things happen. 1: more and more 
people will slowly begin using open source software (linux) until it reaches 
the point that 'everyone' realizes how stupid the concept of proprietary 
software is when compared to open source. 2: proprietary software companies 
get laws passed which mandate trusted computing modules to be fully 
implemented on all computers, these same companies become the final judge on 
what is 'trusted' (rather then letting users judge for themselves) and open 
source software dies because there is no longer any hardware being made that 
it will run on anymore.

                                                             Desktop Linux
hurts Microsoft
Linux has kept a big chunk of the server business out of Microsoft?s hands.
But in 2008, Linux will hurt Microsoft on the desktop. Here?s how.
A new computing platform
Thanks to Moore?s Law and evolving application needs, a new computing 
platform
arrives every 10 years. Mainframes in the ?50s, minicomputers in the ?60s, 
PCs
in the ?70s, microcontrollers in the ?80s, PDAs and cell phones in the ?90s 
and
now sub-$400 - soon to be sub-$300 notebooks.
Small and light enough to be carried everywhere, these sub-notes provide
Internet access, PDA functionality and basic mail and document creation
functionality at a rock-bottom price. Asustek is expected to build 1,000,000
Eee sub-notes in Q1 ?08 alone. Asustek?s competitors are just getting warmed
up.
What can Microsoft do?
Microsoft has gotten fat on the $50 Windows tax it charges PC manufacturers.
But on a razor-thin margin vendors can?t afford Windows.
So they?re going with Linux. If Asustek sells 5 million Eee?s, and their
competitors sell another 5 million, several million consumers will be
introduced to desktop Linux for the first time.
And millions of copies of Windows and Office won?t be sold.
Microsoft will skate in ?08
For all the attention Apple gets for its growing market share, the 
Linux-based
sub-$400 notebook/sub-$200 desktop unit sales will be several times as large 
in
2008. Even combined this won?t hurt Microsoft in 2008, thanks to the growing
migration to Vista.
2009 is a different story. 25 million Linux desktops will take a bite out of
Microsoft sales - one that Wall Street will certainly notice.
The Storage Bits take
Microsoft?s days as a de facto monopoly are coming to an end - and not a
moment too soon. Increasingly desperate attacks on Linux will intensify, but
how does Microsoft go after Wal-Mart?
Comments welcome, of course. How should Microsoft respond to very-low-cost
Linux systems?

	<86d4rhyjvm.fsf_-_@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <643ed6820801311124s6665d729x206f63b960c38070@mail.gmail.com>

We had similar problems in our cul-de-sac with a NetGear music player
getting bumped off all too easily (seems to be an issue with that piece of
gear).  We tried different channels, but reception for internet radio was
getting dropped all too frequently.

What worked better was putting one of those paper/aluminum foil reflectors
on the  Music player box, pointing to the router.
http://www.freeantennas.com/projects/template2/index.html

Ultimately we went with a powerline ethernet adaptor as streaming was too
erratic for our ripped music.  But the antenna eliminated ~80% of the radio
station drops or disconnects.

We also moved the router further away from the front of the house and cable
modem.

The netgear box sensed 3-5 networks almost any day.

Robert

On 31 Jan 2008 09:39:41 -0800, Russell Senior <russell at personaltelco.net>
wrote:

> >>>>> "kurt" == kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> writes:
>
> kurt> One weird thing about wireless in my area: if metro-fi goes
> kurt> down, everything else seems to go down. I've tested this several
> kurt> times with 4 separate laptops, on several different
> kurt> occasions. This is probably a separate topic, but here is a
> kurt> scenario: I check the AP's on 2 - 4 different laptops, Metro-fi
> kurt> and Metro-fi premium are up. Metro-fi goes down, and new AP's
> kurt> become visible (confirmed on multiple laptops). Right now, for
> kurt> example, Metro-fi and Metro-fi premium are up (which are
> kurt> apparently the same AP). If I connect to Metro-fi, eventually it
> kurt> will go down, then 2 - 4 new access points will go up, in
> kurt> addition to Metro-fi, yet, Metro-fi will not deliver
> kurt> internet. Moral of the story is, maybe there is some frequency
> kurt> issue going on in my area, but I'm not sure how to test for that
> kurt> (really want to learn though, so point me to a reference or
> kurt> tool-set if you know of one). If there is a frequency issue, I
> kurt> may not need a new router. I would like to try a Linksys though,
> kurt> as I've never had any trouble with them.
>
> That sounds a lot like an interference problem.  There are only three
> non-overlapping channels: 1, 6, and 11.  That means the some (though
> not much) of the power transmitted on channel 1 will be audible on
> channel 5.  Naturally, the closer you are to the transmit channel, the
> more this cross-channel interference you'll hear.  That fact pushes
> most, but not all people on to 1, 6 and 11.  Some people operate on 3
> or 9 or some other channel because it lets them hear their own AP
> better amongst the noise, while generating noise for two of their
> neighboring "non-overlapping" channels.
>
> As for checking for interference, my first knee-jerk suggestion is
> kismet.  It will show you which APs are present on which channels, and
> also which ones are audibly active from your location.
>
> Kismet[1] (properly configured) will show you 802.11 traffic.  There
> are other users of 2.4GHz spectrum though.  Like, lots.  There are
> cordless phones, microwaves, bluetooth, etc, etc.  One tool that
> purports to provide information on all of that is something called a
> wi-spy[2].  It is a small USB dongle and software that will generate a
> plot of rf energy by frequency across the unlicensed 2.4 GHz band.
> Personal Telco has talked about buying one of these at various times,
> but hasn't.  Partly because one of our members has one already and
> we've been able to borrow it in a pinch.  But, having to borrow it,
> means that it isn't readily at hand, and we've got to have sufficient
> "activation energy"[3] to follow through on it.  They used to be
> reasonably cheap (around $100), but recently about doubled in price.
> You can get one with a detachable antenna for about $400 now.
>
> [1] http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kismet_%28software%29
> [2] http://www.metageek.net/products/wi-spy24x
> [3] http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Activation_energy
>
>
> --
> Russell Senior, Secretary
> russell at personaltelco.net
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
>

On Jan 30, 2008 8:06 AM, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
> Today's question: Would I really notice the difference if I got a new
> router that supports 100M/Full duplex on the WAN side as well as the LAN
> side?

Right now, Comcast is offering 12Mbps bursts on their basic 8Mbps
downstream connections (if you don't know what you have, then you have
this).  You'll probably notice some difference going to full duplex.
While 100Mbps is severe overkill for the application, 10Mbps won't let
you get all you can get out of the burst rate.

> And, which routers do you folks prefer? I'll consider adding wireless
> capability, too.

802.11(b) should offer enough throughput that there shouldn't be a
bottleneck at the cable mode, and has a slightly larger usable signal
radius on the same transmitter and antenna.  (g) offers more speed,
though if you're having signal problems on g, try b instead; it might
make a difference.

-- 
Paul Johnson
baloo at ursine.ca

On 30 Jan 2008 09:05:53 -0800, Russell Senior <russell at personaltelco.net> wrote:
> >>>>> "Richard" == Richard C Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> writes:
>
> Richard> And, which routers do you folks prefer? I'll consider adding
> Richard> wireless capability, too.
>
> For me, the ability to run my own linux-based firmware on the thing is
> paramount, so I would pick something with that in mind.  Of those, I'd
> go for one of these:
>
>   Linksys WRT54GL
>   ASUS WL-500g Premium

I strongly advise against selecting a router that is not compatable
with dd-wrt, just in case you decide the stock firmware is garbage (it
probably is).  Of those, I advise against consumer-grade units; go
with the commercial grade hardware.  You have more flexability in what
you can do with a signal on the commercial units (like throw on
different kinds of antennas to meet signal requirements, etc), and
it's not as likely to randomly peter out for no obvious reason like
the El Cheapo stuff you find at Best Buy or Fry's.


-- 
Paul Johnson
baloo at ursine.ca

Robert Citek wrote:
> On Jan 30, 2008 5:14 PM, Jason Van Cleve <Jason.VanCleve at chrome.com> wrote:
>   
>> Java applications have a couple of file-system references at their
>> disposal:  the current working directory (cwd) and the "classpath".

you can load any resource that you can stuff into a JAR file from the 
classpath - manifest.mf files are common, but i've used classpath 
loading for images, xml, ddl, all sorts of plain text. If i remember 
correctly (it's been ~18 months since i coded Java) most file io classes 
let you load directly from the classpath.

>>>>> "Kristian" == Kristian Erik Hermansen <kristian.hermansen at gmail.com> writes:

Kristian> On Jan 28, 2008 3:09 PM, <jeffw at pdxlug.org> wrote:
>> Thought this was an interesting rebuttal to the Guttman paper:
>> 
>> Linkname: Can Intelligence Agencies Read Overwritten Data?  URL:
>> http://www.nber.org/sys-admin/overwritten-data-guttman.html
>> 
>> Especially liked the Nixon tape example.

Kristian> It's an excellent article.  I hope that the information can
Kristian> proliferate more widely than this list though

My impression of a quick skim was that the "rebuttal" was filled with
(paraphrasing) "I couldn't find a reference" and "I can't think how
that could work".  That's a kind of negative evidence, it raises
doubts but it doesn't prove that recovery is impossible.

Real mechanisms tend to have real artifacts.  I am reminded of a story
about how the US for a time was able to identify individual russian
submarines radioing home from mid-ocean by means of the characteristic
waveform of the telegraph (or whatever you call that in radio) key
transient.  The listeners had a position from the triangulation of
directional receiver stations, and they had a submarine identity from
the key transient, and could therefore track the russian submarine
movements over time on that basis.

That doesn't mean that disk sector contents can be recovered after
being overwritten.  But it also doesn't mean that just because you
can't think of how it could work, that it can't.  You might just not
be thinking hard enough or might be assuming something that's wrong.
The Russians couldn't think of how the US could figure out which
submarine was talking either, but that didn't stop it from happening.

At least, according to the story.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

Most packages that claim they "need" gcc <= 3.x aren't very well 
maintained. It's more likely they've used language constructs that crash 
the stricter new compilers than it is that there's a performance/code 
generation problem that requires an older compiler.

So ... try it with the latest compiler and beat up on the maintainer(s) 
to get with the program! :) P.S.: Gentoo has a nice way of handling 
multiple versions of gcc:

$ l /usr/portage/sys-devel/gcc
ChangeLog              gcc-3.2.3-r4.ebuild  gcc-4.1.2.ebuild
Manifest               gcc-3.3.6-r1.ebuild  gcc-4.2.0.ebuild
files/                 gcc-3.4.6-r2.ebuild  gcc-4.2.1.ebuild
gcc-2.95.3-r10.ebuild  gcc-4.0.3.ebuild     gcc-4.2.2.ebuild
gcc-2.95.3-r9.ebuild   gcc-4.0.4.ebuild     metadata.xml
gcc-3.1.1-r2.ebuild    gcc-4.1.0-r1.ebuild
gcc-3.2.2.ebuild       gcc-4.1.1-r3.ebuild

But I don't know how many of these will actually install. I think I 
tried to put up 2.95 once because gForth claimed better performance with 
it, and found out it wouldn't install. This is a 64-bit box, however, so 
it may work on 32-bit machines.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Thu Jan 31 19:58:48 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Thu, 31 Jan 2008 19:58:48 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Copy/Paste and Java
Message-ID: <20080131195848.cdc28d32.johnxj@comcast.net>

I am continuing to troubleshoot problems with TreeForm, a syntax tree generator program written in Java. Thanks to the developer and help here I have it down to just a couple remaining issues on my Gutsy x86_64 computer. Of these, the most annoying is that copy and paste from TreeForm to other applications fails completely. I have tried all kinds of applications, from word processors to graphics apps, and evidently TreeForm's copy command is not placing anything on the Linux clipboard(s).

The developer told me the solution was on this page.

http://java.sun.com/developer/technicalArticles/Programming/linux/

To save people time running off to the above link, I have copied and pasted below what it says about copy and paste with Java:

---------------
Desktop Differences (Copy and Paste)

If you have been using Windows or a keyboard with a copy and paste key, you might be wondering how to copy and paste text between Java programs and other desktop programs and terminals.

Linux uses a mouse-driven copy and paste mechanism where mouse button one selects and copies text, and mouse button two pastes the text. This technique works for Abstract Window Toolkit (AWT) components because they use the primary selection to achieve copy and paste. Project Swing components, however, use the system clipboard for copy and paste, and most tools on the desktop, apart from the Netscape browser, do not use the clipboard.

A workaround to this limitation is to map a key or mouse button to access the system clipboard as follows:

*VT100.Translations: #override \
            <Btn3Up>:                select-end(CLIPBOARD) \n\
            <Btn2Up>:                insert-selection(CLIPBOARD) \n

The above lines can be passed as a value to an X tool using the -xrm option. Alternatively, the mapping can be made accessible to the entire desktop by including them in the .Xdefaults file in the user's home directory. The command xrdb -merge $HOME/.Xdefaults will reload updates in the .Xdefaults file. 
--------------

The problem is that the above is not comprehensible to me.

First, is the above correct?

Second, if it is correct, what do I do? I don't understand things like "the above lines can be passed as a value to an X tool using the -xrm option." I need instructions for a non-programmer who only dimly understands that Java is a programming language.

I did figure I'd start by adding the lines to the .Xdefaults file in my home folder, but said file does not exist. I opened Text Editor and pasted in the above lines, then saved it as ~/.Xdefaults, and then I did the above xrdb ... command. Still nothing - copying in TreeForm places nothing on the clipboard.

Any suggestions welcome.

From jason at vancleve.com  Thu Jan 31 17:40:26 2008
From: jason at vancleve.com (Jason Van Cleve)
Date: Fri, 01 Feb 2008 01:40:26 -0000
Subject: [PLUG] Command line and paths
In-Reply-To: <4145b6790801311356i6f330d19t4cfbca3b0dd3fa1d@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20080130000042.c08839d1.johnxj@comcast.net><c834ec80801300024w164f6f26l9a0d369f2729359a@mail.gmail.com><20080130090909.2475af82.johnxj@comcast.net><1DEC803BC73785498C98EF74AA6ECFEA01AC66B2@pdxmsx01.chrome.dt.inc>
	<4145b6790801311356i6f330d19t4cfbca3b0dd3fa1d@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <02de01c86470$4fb62130$810817ac@chrome.dt.inc>

That's its primary function, yes, for loading Java modules required by the
application.  As Joe points out, though, Java can load any sort of file from
the classpath.  (There are stream abtractions that apply to all files,
whether they're loaded via the classpath or using a file descriptor, a URI,
etc.)  And in fact, the classpath may contain directories as well as JAR
files, and Java will search both (in the order in which they appear in the
classpath) for class modules and other files.

The classpath can be passed in the Java command line, it can be defined as
the "CLASSPATH" environment variable, and for executable JARs, it can be
defined in a "manifest" file.  Moreover, the "JAVA_HOME" environment
variable is sometimes used to find the active Java installation.  Juggling
these two variables along with the system "PATH" has caused way too many
headaches, especially for linux users, who are actually concerned about
versions.

If you're wrestling with multiple Java installations in linux, consider this
advice:  don't both with system-wide CLASSPATH and JAVA_HOME environment
variables, and eschew java-configuration tools that create them.  They'll
only get in your way.  Just add to your PATH the ./bin directory in your
latest Java installation, and leave it at that.  Define the classpath (and
JAVA_HOME, where it's necessary) only in local script wrappers.

I've used gentoo's java-config tool, for example, and it's caused me a
ridiculous lot of problems.  Managing Java installations just isn't that
complicated.

--Jason
